Salah — Prayer
intermediate
12 min
Responsive Dhikr in Prayer: Intra-Hanafi Dispute and the Exception of Notification Tasbih
Continues discussion on the third category of speech in prayer with sub-branches and the exception of notification tasbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on responsive dhikr during prayer and explain why notification tasbih is an exception that cannot be analogized.

Core Idea
Responsive dhikr during prayer invalidates it, except notification tasbih — a textual exception that cannot be analogized.

In this lesson, we continue our study of speech in prayer, focusing on the third category: dhikr uttered as a response to external events. The source lists specific examples and presents the intra-Hanafi disagreement between the Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf. We will trace their proofs and understand why the notification tasbih is a unique exception, fixed by a hadith and not extendable to other cases.

Guided Path
1
List the disputed actions
Identify the examples given: saying 'Jalla Jalaluhu', sending blessings upon hearing the Prophet's name, tasbih upon thunder/lightning, expressing joy, tashmit (responding to a sneeze), and istirja'. Note the common feature: all are dhikr uttered as a response to something external.
These are distinct from the original ruling of prompting someone other than one's imam, where Abu Yusuf agrees on invalidation.
2
State the Sahibayn position and proof
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that all these invalidate the prayer. Their proof is the general principle from the hadith 'Each person gets what he intends' – since the speaker intends to respond, the dhikr becomes speech (kalam) in meaning.
They view the intention as the decisive factor transforming dhikr into speech.
3
State the Abu Yusuf position and proof
Abu Yusuf holds that these do not invalidate because the words themselves are dhikr, and intention does not change their essence. His proof is the hadith permitting tasbih for notification, which shows that even when intended as a signal, tasbih remains valid dhikr.
Abu Yusuf prioritizes the linguistic form over the intention.
4
Analyze the exception for notification tasbih
The hadith 'If something happens to one of you, let him say tasbih' provides a specific dispensation. The qiyas would require invalidation, but the hadith overrides it. This exception is limited and cannot be analogized to other cases.
The term used in the text is 'ustuniyya' – a pillar-like exception fixed by text.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Ayni in al-Binaya authenticates the Sahibayn view. Therefore, the relied-upon position for fatwa is that responsive dhikr invalidates the prayer, except for notification tasbih which is valid by consensus.
Abu Yusuf's disagreement is only in these specific sub-branches; he agrees with the Sahibayn on the original ruling of prompting non-imam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتتفرع عليه فروع منها : إنه لو سمع اسم الله فقال في الصلاة : جلّ جلاله، أو سمع اسم النبي صلى عليه، أو سمع صوت رعد أو رأى برقاً فسبح، أو سمع خبراً يسره أو نحوه ذلك، ومن هذا الجنس مسألة التشميت والاسترجاع وغيرهما مما مر ذكره ، ففي هذه الصور تفسد الصلاة عندهما لا عند أبي يوسف الله، وصحح العيني في «البناية» قولهما في هذا الباب.
فإن قلت: لو استأذن المصلّى أحد فسبح إعلاماً له لا تفسد صلاته اتفاقاً ؛ لحديث : «إذا نابت أحدكم نائبة فليسبح ) ، أخرجه أصحاب السنن والصحاح، وهذا يؤيد قول أبي يوسف الله من أنّ الذكر لا يتغيّر بعزيمة، وما الفارق عندهما في هذه المسألة وفي المسائل السابقة حيث قالا بالفساد في السابقة دون هذه؟
قلت : مقتضى القياس هاهنا أيضاً الفساد أخذاً من حديث : «لكل امرئ ما نوى»، فإذا نوى بذكر الخطاب أو الجواب أو الكلام خرج من كونه ذكراً محضاً ، لكنّه ترك هذا القياس في هذه المسألة ؛ لورود الأثر، فلا يقاس عليه غيره.
وليعلم أن خلاف أبي يوسف له إنما ذكروه في المسائل التي ذكرناها، وأما في مسألة الفتح على غير إمامه فلم يذكر في عامة الكتب خلافه فيها، وقال في «الذخيرة»: قال بعض مشايخنا ما ذكر من الجواب فيما إذا أراد التعليم يجب أن يكون قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد الله ، وأما على قول أبي يوسف الله ، فينبغي أن لا يفسد ؛ لأنه قرآن، فلا يتغير بقصد القارئ». انتهى. وفي فتح القدير»: «أقرب ما ينقصُ كلامه ما وافق عليه أبو يوسف له من الفساد بالفتح على غير إمامه فهو قرآن ، وقد تغيّر إلى وقوع الفساد به بالعزيمة». انتهى. وفي «غنية المستملي»: «أفاد قول الكمال لله له له أقرب ما ينقض به ... . ... الخ أنّ قول أبي يوسف له قد ينقض بغيره أيضاً، وهو ما ذكره قاضي خان له في «فتاواه»(٢) من أنه لو سبح التفت
And there branch off from it branches, including: if he hears the name of Allah and says in prayer: 'Jalla Jalaluhu', or hears the name of the Prophet and sends blessings upon him, or hears thunder or sees lightning and says tasbih, or hears pleasing news or the like, and from this category are the matters of tashmit (responding to sneeze) and istirja' (saying inna lillah) and others that have been mentioned. In these cases, prayer invalidates according to them (Sahibayn) but not according to Abu Yusuf. And al-Ayni authenticated their view in al-Binaya. If it is said: If someone seeks permission to enter and the person praying says tasbih as a notification, his prayer does not invalidate by consensus, due to the hadith: 'If something happens to one of you, let him say tasbih' — recorded by the authors of Sunan and Sahih. This supports the statement of Abu Yusuf that dhikr is not changed by intention. What is the difference according to them in this case and the previous cases where they said invalidation in the former and not in this? I say: The analogical reasoning here would also require invalidation, based on the hadith 'Each person gets what he intends'. If he intends by his dhikr address or response or speech, it ceases to be pure dhikr. But this analogy was abandoned in this case due to the textual evidence, so nothing else is analogized to it. And let it be known that the disagreement of Abu Yusuf is only mentioned in the issues we have mentioned. As for the issue of prompting other than one's imam, no disagreement from him is mentioned in most books. It is said in al-Dhakhira: Some of our scholars said: what was mentioned regarding the response when he intends teaching, it must be the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. As for the statement of Abu Yusuf, it should not invalidate because it is Quran, and it is not changed by the reader's intention. End. In Fath al-Qadir: 'The nearest thing that contradicts his statement is what Abu Yusuf agreed upon regarding invalidation by prompting other than one's imam, for it is Quran, and it changed to invalidation by intention.' End. In Ghunyat al-Mustamli: 'The statement of al-Kamal... that the statement of Abu Yusuf may be contradicted by other things, and that is what Qadi Khan mentioned in his Fatawa that if he says tasbih and turns...'
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion from the previous lesson on the third category of speech in prayer. The author gives specific examples of actions that fall under the disputed third category: saying 'Jalla Jalaluhu' upon hearing Allah's name, sending blessings upon the Prophet when his name is mentioned, saying tasbih upon thunder or lightning, expressing joy at good news, and also the cases of tashmit (saying 'Yarhamuk Allah' to a sneezer) and istirja' (saying 'Inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un' upon hearing bad news). According to Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani), all these invalidate the prayer because although the words are dhikr, they are uttered as a response to external events, making them speech in meaning. According to Abu Yusuf, they do not invalidate because the words themselves are dhikr. The author notes that al-Ayni in al-Binaya authenticated the view of Sahibayn as the correct one. Then a question is posed: If someone seeks permission to enter and the person praying says tasbih as a notification, why does it not invalidate by consensus? The answer is that this case is excluded by the explicit hadith 'If something happens to one of you, let him say tasbih.' The analogy would require invalidation because the tasbih is intended as a response, but the hadith overrides the analogy. So this is a specific exception and cannot be analogized to other cases. Finally, the author clarifies that Abu Yusuf's disagreement is only in the mentioned cases (the third category and its branches). In the original ruling of prompting someone other than one's imam, there is no recorded disagreement from Abu Yusuf; he agrees it invalidates. This is because the words of the prompt are Quranic, but the intention to teach changes their ruling. The passage ends with a quotation from Qadi Khan's Fatawa regarding a case of tasbih and turning around, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
التشميت (al-tashmit): Responding to a sneeze by saying 'Yarhamuk Allah' (may Allah have mercy on you) during prayer.
الاسترجاع (al-istirja'): Saying 'Inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un' (to Allah we belong and to Him we return) upon hearing bad news.
الذكر الجوابي (al-dhikr al-jawabi): Dhikr uttered as a response to an external event, e.g., saying 'Jalla Jalaluhu' upon hearing Allah's name.
تسبيح الإعلام (tasbih al-i'lam): Saying 'Subhan Allah' as a notification to someone, e.g., when someone seeks permission to enter.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning: applying a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the basis for the exception of notification tasbih?
A) Qiyas (analogical reasoning)
B) A specific hadith
C) Ijma (consensus) alone
D) Istihsan (juristic preference)

Key Takeaway
Responsive dhikr during prayer invalidates according to the mufta bihi (Sahibayn), except for notification tasbih which is a textual exception that cannot be analogized.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Voluntary Dhikr in Prayer
Intra-Hanafi dispute on voluntary dhikr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on voluntary dhikr in prayer and explain why the case of tasbih for permission is an exception.

Core Idea
Intention changes dhikr into speech, invalidating prayer unless a specific hadith permits it.

This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute about performing voluntary dhikr in prayer — such as glorifying Allah upon hearing thunder or sending blessings upon hearing the Prophet’s name. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that such dhikr invalidates the prayer because the worshipper intends to respond, turning the remembrance into speech. Abu Yusuf counters that the words themselves remain dhikr regardless of intention. The text resolves the apparent contradiction with the consensus case of tasbih for permission, explaining it as a special exception. This lesson walks through the dispute and the underlying legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cases
List the acts of voluntary dhikr mentioned: saying 'Jallalah' upon hearing Allah's name, sending blessings upon hearing the Prophet's name, tasbih at thunder or lightning, expressing joy at good news, tashmit, istirja'. Recognize that all involve responding to an external event.
These are not part of the prescribed prayer but are prompted by external stimuli.
2
State the ruling of the two
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that the prayer becomes invalid (fasid) in all these cases. Their reasoning: the worshipper intends to respond, and the hadith 'every person will have what he intended' means the dhikr becomes speech, which is prohibited in prayer.
The intention (azimah) changes the legal nature of the act.
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that the prayer remains valid because the words themselves are dhikr and are not transformed by intention. He cites the hadith permitting tasbih for permission as evidence that tasbih remains dhikr even when used to inform.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The text raises an objection: if tasbih for permission does not invalidate by consensus, how can the two claim tasbih invalidates in other cases? The author responds that the case of tasbih for permission is a specific exception (istihsan) based on a direct command from the Prophet, so analogy is abandoned there but not elsewhere.
This is a clear example of istihsan overriding qiyas due to a stronger textual proof.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Ayni in al-Binayah gave preponderance to the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Therefore, the relied-upon position is that voluntary dhikr intended as a response invalidates the prayer, except for tasbih for permission.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتتفرع عليه فروع منها : إنه لو سمع اسم الله فقال في الصلاة : جلّ جلاله، أو سمع اسم النبي صلى عليه، أو سمع صوت رعد أو رأى برقاً فسبح، أو سمع خبراً يسره أو نحوه ذلك، ومن هذا الجنس مسألة التشميت والاسترجاع وغيرهما مما مر ذكره ، ففي هذه الصور تفسد الصلاة عندهما لا عند أبي يوسف الله، وصحح العيني في «البناية» قولهما في هذا الباب.
فإن قلت: لو استأذن المصلّى أحد فسبح إعلاماً له لا تفسد صلاته اتفاقاً؛ لحديث : «إذا نابت أحدكم نائبة فليسبح »، أخرجه أصحاب السنن والصحاح، وهذا يؤيد قول أبي يوسف الله من أنّ الذكر لا يتغيّر بعزيمة، وما الفارق عندهما في هذه المسألة وفي المسائل السابقة حيث قالا بالفساد في السابقة دون هذه؟
قلت : مقتضى القياس هاهنا أيضاً الفساد أخذاً من حديث : «لكل امرئ ما نوى»، فإذا نوى بذكر الخطاب أو الجواب أو الكلام خرج من كونه ذكراً محضاً ، لكنّه ترك هذا القياس في هذه المسألة ؛ لورود الأثر، فلا يقاس عليه غيره.
And from it branch many branches: if he hears the name of Allah and says in prayer: 'Glory be to His Majesty,' or hears the name of the Prophet and sends blessings upon him, or hears thunder or sees lightning and says subhanallah, or hears good news or the like of that, and from this category is the matter of tashmit, istirja', and others that have been mentioned before; in these cases, the prayer is invalid according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), not according to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him. And al-Ayni in al-Binayah gave preponderance to their view in this chapter.
If it is said: If someone asks permission from the one praying and he says subhanallah to inform him, his prayer is not invalid by consensus, based on the hadith: 'When something befalls one of you, let him say subhanallah,' reported by the compilers of the sunan and sahih works. And this supports the view of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, that remembrance (dhikr) is not changed by intention (azimah). So what is the difference according to the two in this matter and in the previous matters, where they said it invalidates in the previous ones but not this one?
I say: The dictate of analogy here as well is invalidation, taken from the hadith: 'Every person will have what he intended.' So when he intends by the remembrance to address, respond, or speak, it leaves being pure remembrance. But this analogy is abandoned in this matter because of the (specific) narration (athar), so nothing else is analogized to it.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about various forms of dhikr performed in prayer that are not part of the prescribed prayer. It lists examples: saying 'Jallalah' upon hearing Allah's name, sending blessings upon hearing the Prophet's name, performing tasbih at thunder or lightning, expressing joy at good news, and also includes the earlier-discussed cases of tashmit (responding to a sneeze) and istirja' (saying 'inna lillah' at a calamity). The ruling according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad is that the prayer becomes invalid (fasid) in all these cases, whereas Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the prayer remains valid. Al-Ayni in al-Binayah preferred the view of the two. The author then raises an objection: there is consensus that if a person seeking permission from the worshipper is responded to with tasbih, the prayer is not invalid. This is supported by a sahih hadith. This seems to support Abu Yusuf's general principle that the nature of dhikr is not changed by intention (azimah). The author answers that the analogy would indeed demand invalidation here as well because the worshipper intends to respond, which changes the dhikr into speech, based on the hadith 'every person will have what he intended.' However, this analogy is set aside (istihsan) in the specific case of tasbih for permission due to the explicit prophetic command. Therefore, that case is an exception and cannot be extended to others.

Key Terms
تشميت (tashmit): Responding to a sneeze by saying 'Yarhamuk Allah'.
استرجاع (istirja'): Saying 'Inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un' upon hearing bad news.
عزيمة (azimah): Determination or intention that changes the nature of the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on saying 'Subhanallah' in prayer upon hearing thunder?
A) The prayer remains valid.
B) The prayer becomes invalid.
C) The prayer is makruh but valid.
D) It is prohibited only if spoken aloud.

Key Takeaway
Intention (niyyah) can change the nature of dhikr in prayer from worship to speech, invalidating the prayer unless a specific textual exception applies.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

Intention and Invalidity in Prompting the Imam
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fath and intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether prompting the imam invalidates the prayer and identify the mufta bihi position based on the prompter's intention.

Core Idea
The intention of the prompter (teaching vs. recitation) determines whether his prayer remains valid.

Prompting the imam during prayer (fath) is a common occurrence, especially when the imam hesitates or recites incorrectly. But does this act invalidate the follower's prayer? The Hanafi school has two views: one considers it invalidating because it is 'meaningful speech,' while the other exempts it due to necessity. The central factor is the intention of the prompter: does he intend to teach the imam or merely to recite Qur'an? This lesson unpacks the dispute and identifies the relied-upon ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Fath is prompting the imam by reciting a verse. In general, any speech during prayer that conveys meaning invalidates it. But there is an exception for tasbih (to alert the imam) and, by extension, for teaching the imam the correct recitation.
Default rule: kalam ma'na invalidates.
2
Note the exception based on necessity
The school permits certain speech when needed. Fath is permitted due to necessity (darurah) to correct the imam. However, if the prompter intends mere address, it remains invalidating.
Necessity changes the ruling.
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf on the prompter's intention
Some scholars (e.g., Mulla al-Hidad) say the prompter intends recitation of Qur'an, which is prohibited but not invalidating. Others (al-Hidayah, Ibn al-Humam) say he intends teaching, which is permissible. The difference affects validity.
Intention is the crux of the dispute.
4
Trace the mufta bihi position
The passage cites that the fatwa is on non-invalidation absolutely for prompting one's own imam. Al-Hidayah corrects that the intention is teaching. Thus, the prayer remains valid when the prompter intends to teach.
Relied-upon view: prayer is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليعلم أن خلاف أبي يوسف له إنما ذكروه في المسائل التي ذكرناها، وأما في مسألة الفتح على غير إمامه فلم يذكر في عامة الكتب خلافه فيها، وقال في «الذخيرة»: قال بعض مشايخنا ما ذكر من الجواب فيما إذا أراد التعليم يجب أن يكون قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد الله ، وأما على قول أبي يوسف الله ، فينبغي أن لا يفسد ؛ لأنه قرآن، فلا يتغير بقصد القارئ». انتهى. وفي فتح القدير»: «أقرب ما ينقصُ كلامه ما وافق عليه أبو يوسف له من الفساد بالفتح على غير إمامه فهو قرآن ، وقد تغيّر إلى وقوع الفساد به بالعزيمة». انتهى. وفي «غنية المستملي»: «أفاد قول الكمال لله له له أقرب ما ينقض به ... . ... الخ أنّ قول أبي يوسف له قد ينقض بغيره أيضاً، وهو ما ذكره قاضي خان له في «فتاواه»(۲) من أنه لو سبح التفت قال بعض المشايخ: إذا قرأ إمامُهُ مقدار ما يجوز به الصلاة، أو انتقل إلى آية أُخرى ففتح تفسد صلاة الفاتح، وإن أخذ الإمام منه تفسد صلاة الإمام أيضاً. وبعضُهم قالوا : لا تفسد في شيء من ذلك ، وسمعتُ أن الفتوى على ذلك (۲) كان عند رجل يسمى بيحيى ، فقال المصلي : يَيَحْيَى خُذِ الْكِتَبَ بِقُوَّةٍ ﴾ (١) ، أو كان هناك رجل مسمّى بموسى فقال : في وَمَا تِلْكَ بِيَمِينِكَ يَمُوسَى (٢) ، إن قصد به قراءة القرآن لا تفسد صلاته بالاتفاق، وإن قصد به الخطاب تفسد في قولهم جميعا». انتهى. وليعلم أنهم اختلفوا في أنّ الفاتح على إمامه هل ينوي التعليم أم قراءة القرآن. فذكر بعضُهم : إنّه ينوي القراءة، وصحح في «الهداية) (۳) وغيرها أن ينوي التعليم ، وحكم ابن الهمام في «الفتح» (4) على القول الأول بالسهو؛ لأنه عدول إلى المنهي عنه عن المرخص فيه، ورجح ملا الهداد الجونفوي في حواشي الهداية القول الأول. وعلله بأن الفتح مفسد في نفسه ؛ لأنه كلام معنى إلا أنه عفى عنه للضرورة، فيجب الاحتراز عنه ما أمكن الاحتراز عنه في النيّة، وإن لم يكن في الفعل، ثم الفتح في نفسه مفسد ومنهي . عنه، والتلاوة في نفسها منهيّة، وليست بمفسدة فالنية ما هو منهي وليس بمفسد أولى من نيّة الفتح الذي هو منهي عنه ومفسد.
فإن قلت : الفتح مرخص فيه والقراءة منهي عنها.
قلت : من ضرورة الرخصة بالفتح الرخصة بالتلاوة ؛ لعدم تصوّر الفتح بدونها ، فكان كل من التلاوة والفتح مرخصاً فيه». انتهى ملخصاً.
[١] قوله : لا تفسد ; نعم ينبغي للإمام أن لا يلجئ المقتدي إلى الفتح، بل يركع إن كان قرأ قدر ما تجوز به الصلاة، أو ينتقل إلى آية أخرى، فإن أحوج إلى ذلك بأن وقف ساكتاً أو مكرراً ولم يركع ولم ينتقل كره , وكذا يكره للمقتدي أن يعجل في الفتح لم يلجئه الإمام. كذا في «الغنية» و«فتاوى قاضي خان»(٥). [٢]قوله : على ذلك ; أي على عدم الفساد مطلقاً في الفتح على إمامه ; لما تدل ما
And it should be known that they mentioned the disagreement of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, only in the matters we have mentioned. As for the matter of fath (prompting) to other than his imam, no disagreement of his is mentioned in most books. He said in al-Dhakhirah: 'Some of our teachers said: the answer that has been mentioned regarding when he intends teaching must be the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them. As for according to the view of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, it should not invalidate because it is Qur'an; it does not change by the intention of the reader.' End. And in Fath al-Qadir: 'The closest thing that spoils his speech is what Abu Yusuf agreed upon regarding invalidation by fath to other than his imam, because it is Qur'an, but it has changed to causing invalidation by intention (azimah).' End. And in Ghunyat al-Mustamli: 'The statement of al-Kamal, may Allah have mercy on him, indicated that ... the view of Abu Yusuf may also be spoiled by something else, and that is what Qadi Khan mentioned in his Fatawa: that if he says subhanallah and turns ... Some teachers said: If his imam recites an amount that suffices for the prayer, or moves to another verse, and he prompts, the prayer of the prompter is invalid; and if the imam took from him, the prayer of the imam is also invalid. And some said: it does not invalidate in any of that. And I heard that the fatwa is on that (i.e., no invalidation). And if there was a man named Yahya, and the worshipper said: 'O Yahya, take the book with strength,' or there was a man named Musa, and he said: 'And what is that in your right hand, O Musa?' if he intended thereby recitation of Qur'an, his prayer is not invalid by agreement; and if he intended address, it invalidates according to all of them.' End. And it should be known that they differed concerning the prompter to his imam: whether he intends teaching (ta'lim) or recitation of Qur'an. Some mentioned that he intends recitation, and it was corrected in al-Hidayah and others that he intends teaching. And Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath ruled the first view to be erroneous, because it is turning from the permissible to the prohibited. And Mulla al-Hidad al-Jawnfawi in his glosses on al-Hidayah gave preponderance to the first view. He reasoned that fath in itself is invalidating because it is meaningful speech, except it was excused due to necessity. So it is obligatory to avoid it as much as possible in intention, even if not in action. And then fath in itself is invalidating and prohibited; recitation (tilawah) in itself is prohibited (i.e., when not part of prayer) and not invalidating. So intending what is prohibited but not invalidating is better than intending what is both prohibited and invalidating.
If it is said: Fath is permissible (murakhkhas fihi) and recitation is prohibited.
I say: From the necessity of the permission for fath comes permission for recitation, because fath cannot be conceived without it. So both recitation and fath are permissible.' End summarized.
[1] His saying: 'does not invalidate' – Yes, it is appropriate for the imam not to force the follower to prompt; rather, he should bow if he has recited enough to make the prayer valid, or move to another verse. If he causes need for that by remaining silent or repeating, and does not bow or move, it is disliked. Similarly, it is disliked for the follower to hasten to prompt before the imam causes it. So in al-Ghunya and Fatawa Qadi Khan. [2] His saying: 'on that' – i.e., on the non-invalidation absolutely in prompting his imam, because it indicates that...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage continues the intra-Hanafi discussion, now focused on the specific issue of 'fath' (prompting the imam). It first clarifies that the earlier disagreement of Abu Yusuf does not apply to the case of prompting someone other than one's own imam; most books do not mention his disagreement there. Several later sources are quoted: al-Dhakhirah states that according to Abu Yusuf, prompting with Qur'an should not invalidate because the intention does not change the Quranic nature. Fath al-Qadir points out that Abu Yusuf actually agreed to invalidation in the case of prompting a non-imam, though it is Qur'an; the intention makes it invalid. Ghunyat al-Mustamli indicates that Abu Yusuf's view might be inconsistent. Then a scenario from Qadi Khan's Fatawa is cited: if the imam has recited enough or moved to another verse and the follower prompts, some say the prompter's prayer is invalid and if the imam accepts it, his also; others say it does not invalidate. The relied-upon fatwa, according to the author, is that it does not invalidate absolutely when prompting one's own imam. An example is given: if a man named Yahya is present and the worshipper recites the verse 'O Yahya, take the book,' intending recitation, prayer is valid by consensus; if intending address, it invalidates. Then a key ikhtilaf is discussed: what does the prompter intend (teaching or recitation)? According to al-Hidayah, the correct view is teaching (ta'lim), which is permissible due to necessity. Ibn al-Humam considered the alternative (recitation) erroneous. However, Mulla al-Hidad preferred the first view (recitation), reasoning that fath is inherently invalidating (as 'meaningful speech') and excused only by necessity, so one should avoid it as much as possible in intention; intending recitation (which is prohibited but not invalidating) is better than intending the invalidating act. The author adds a rejoinder: if one argues that fath is permissible while recitation is prohibited, the response is that the permission for fath necessarily includes permission for the recitation needed for it, so both are permissible.

Key Terms
الفتح (al-fath): Prompting the imam by reciting a verse or part of a verse to correct or continue the recitation.
التعليم (al-ta'lim): The intention of teaching the imam, which makes the act an instruction rather than idle speech.
النيّة (al-niyyah): Intention that determines the ruling of the act; in fath, it changes whether the act is invalidating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon view) in this passage, what is the ruling when a follower prompts his imam with the intention of teaching?
A) The follower's prayer becomes invalid.
B) The follower's prayer remains valid.
C) The imam's prayer becomes invalid.
D) Both prayers become invalid.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, prompting the imam (fath) does not invalidate the follower's prayer, as the intention is assumed to be teaching (ta'lim), and necessity permits it.

Ruling on Prompting the Imam (Fath) in Prayer
Hanafi internal dispute on fatah and intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two Hanafi positions on prompting the imam during prayer, identify the mufta bihi ruling, and explain how intention (teaching vs recitation) affects the validity.

Core Idea
Prompting the imam does not invalidate prayer; the follower should intend teaching, not recitation.

The act of a follower prompting the imam during prayer by reciting a verse aloud has been a subject of disagreement among Hanafi scholars. This passage presents two earlier opinions on whether it invalidates the prayer, then delves into the more nuanced dispute about the intention behind the prompting. Understanding these rulings helps clarify the boundaries of speech in prayer and the role of necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions on invalidation
Some early mashayikh said prompting invalidates the prayer of both follower and imam; others said it does not. Note that the fatwa is on non-invalidation.
This shows the school's practical ruling despite theoretical differences.
2
Analyze the consensus case using Quranic verses
If a worshipper recites a verse containing a direct address (like 'O Yahya') and intends to address a person by that name, the prayer is invalidated by consensus.
The key is intention: Quranic recitation vs. addressing a person.
3
Explore the dispute on the prompter's intention
Later scholars differed: some held the prompter intends recitation (which is prohibited but not invalidating), while others held he intends teaching (which is permitted).
Al-Hidayah prefers teaching; Ibn al-Humam considered recitation-intention as forgetfulness.
4
Evaluate the arguments for each view
Mulla al-Hidad argued that fatah is inherently invalidating but pardoned; thus recitation (prohibited but not invalidating) is a lesser evil to intend. Others responded that both fatah and recitation are permitted by necessity.
This dialectic (qaala-qultu) illustrates the method of Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال بعض المشايخ: إذا قرأ إمامُهُ مقدار ما يجوز به الصلاة، أو انتقل إلى آية أُخرى ففتح تفسد صلاة الفاتح، وإن أخذ الإمام منه تفسد صلاة الإمام أيضاً. وبعضُهم قالوا : لا تفسد في شيء من ذلك ، وسمعتُ أن الفتوى على ذلك (۲) كان عند رجل يسمى بيحيى ، فقال المصلي : يَيَحْيَى خُذِ الْكِتَبَ بِقُوَّةٍ ﴾ (١) ، أو كان هناك رجل مسمّى بموسى فقال : في وَمَا تِلْكَ بِيَمِينِكَ يَمُوسَى (٢) ، إن قصد به قراءة القرآن لا تفسد صلاته بالاتفاق، وإن قصد به الخطاب تفسد في قولهم جميعا». انتهى. وليعلم أنهم اختلفوا في أنّ الفاتح على إمامه هل ينوي التعليم أم قراءة القرآن. فذكر بعضُهم : إنّه ينوي القراءة، وصحح في «الهداية) (۳) وغيرها أن ينوي التعليم ، وحكم ابن الهمام في «الفتح» (4) على القول الأول بالسهو؛ لأنه عدول إلى المنهي عنه عن المرخص فيه، ورجح ملا الهداد الجونفوي في حواشي الهداية القول الأول. وعلله بأن الفتح مفسد في نفسه ؛ لأنه كلام معنى إلا أنه عفى عنه للضرورة، فيجب الاحتراز عنه ما أمكن الاحتراز عنه في النيّة، وإن لم يكن في الفعل، ثم الفتح في نفسه مفسد ومنهي . عنه، والتلاوة في نفسها منهيّة، وليست بمفسدة فالنية ما هو منهي وليس بمفسد أولى من نيّة الفتح الذي هو منهي عنه ومفسد. فإن قلت : الفتح مرخص فيه والقراءة منهي عنها. قلت : من ضرورة الرخصة بالفتح الرخصة بالتلاوة ؛ لعدم تصوّر الفتح بدونها ، فكان كل من التلاوة والفتح مرخصاً فيه». انتهى ملخصاً.
Some mashayikh said: If the imam recited what is sufficient for the prayer, or moved to another verse, and the follower prompted (fataha), the prayer of the prompter is invalidated; and if he took from the imam, the prayer of the imam is also invalidated. And some of them said: It is not invalidated in any of that. And I heard that the fatwa is upon that (non-invalidation). There was a man named Yahya, so the praying person said: 'O Yahya, take the book with strength!' (Quran 19:12), or there was a man named Musa, so he said: 'And what is that in your right hand, O Musa?' (Quran 20:17): if he intended by it the recitation of the Quran, his prayer is not invalidated by consensus; and if he intended by it addressing (the person), it is invalidated in all of their opinions. End. And let it be known that they differed concerning the one who prompts (al-fatih) his imam: does he intend teaching or recitation of the Quran? Some of them mentioned that he intends recitation, and it was declared correct in al-Hidayah and others that he intends teaching. Ibn al-Humam ruled in al-Fath that the first opinion (intending recitation) is forgetfulness (sahw), because it is turning from the permitted to the prohibited. Mulla al-Hidad al-Jawnfuri in his glosses on al-Hidayah preferred the first opinion. He explained it by saying: The fatḥ (prompting) is invalidating in itself, because it is speech with meaning, except that it has been pardoned due to necessity; so it is obligatory to guard against it as much as possible in intention, even if not in action. Then the fatḥ is itself invalidating and prohibited, and the recitation is itself prohibited and not invalidating, so intending what is prohibited and not invalidating is better than intending the fatḥ which is prohibited and invalidating. If you say: The fatḥ is permitted and recitation is prohibited. I say: From the necessity of permission for the fatḥ comes permission for recitation, because the fatḥ cannot be imagined without it, so both recitation and fatḥ are permitted. End of summary.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a follower prompting the imam (الفتح على الإمام) during prayer. It begins by presenting two opposing opinions among later Hanafi scholars: one holds that prompting invalidates the prayer of the prompter (and possibly the imam), while the other holds it does not invalidate at all. The author states that the fatwa is upon the latter. Then a specific case is given using the verses about Yahya and Musa: if the person intends to recite Quran, the prayer is valid; if he intends to address the person named, it invalidates by consensus. Next, the passage delves into the intention of the one who prompts: some say he intends recitation, but according to al-Hidayah, he should intend teaching. Ibn al-Humam considers the first intention (recitation) to be forgetful because it turns from the permitted (teaching) to the prohibited (recitation). Mullā Hijād al-Jawnfūrī prefers the first opinion, arguing that the act of prompting is inherently invalidating since it is speech with meaning (kalām maʿnā), but it is excused for necessity. So one should avoid it in intention as much as possible. He further argues that prompting is both prohibited and invalidating, while recitation is prohibited but not invalidating, so intending recitation is better. A possible objection is that prompting is permitted while recitation is prohibited, but the response is that the permission for prompting necessitates permission for recitation, as the former cannot occur without the latter. Thus both are permitted in this context.

Key Terms
الفتح (al-fatḥ): Promting the imam by reciting a verse or phrase when the imam pauses or is silent.
الفتح على الإمام (al-fatḥ ʿalā al-imām): The act of a follower alerting the imam by reciting aloud, especially when the imam has stopped reciting.
السهو (al-sahw): Forgetfulness; in this context, Ibn al-Humam considered the intention to recite as a forgetful action (turning from permitted to prohibited).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a person prompts the imam and intends to recite the Quran, the prayer is:
A) invalidated by consensus
B) not invalidated
C) invalidated only if the imam stops
D) disliked but valid

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position is that prompting the imam does not invalidate the prayer, and the follower should intend to teach, not to recite Quran.

Reading from the Quran (Mushaf) During Prayer: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute over mushaf recitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on reciting from a mushaf during prayer, identify the proofs of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the relied-upon view.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: reciting from a mushaf invalidates prayer; the Sahibayn: it is only disliked.

This lesson examines a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute: whether reciting from a physical copy of the Quran (mushaf) during prayer invalidates the act. The disagreement turns on the nature of the act—does it constitute external learning (talaqqun min al-kharij) or is it an extension of worship? Understanding both positions and the final tarjih is essential for correct practice.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify that Abu Hanifa rules reciting from a mushaf invalidates the prayer (mufsid). Note that this is the relied-upon view.
The text says: 'وهذا هو الصحيح كما في الكافي وغيره'.
2
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that it does not invalidate but is makruh tanzih (disliked).
The text says: 'وعندهما القراءة من المصحف غير مفسدة، بل مكروهة'.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's proof
They rely on the practice of Dhakwan leading Aisha in Ramadan while reading from a mushaf, and the argument that looking at the mushaf is an act of worship.
The text narrates: 'أن ذكوان مولى عائشة ... يقرأ من المصحف'.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's proof and response
Abu Hanifa cites the hadith from Ibn Abbas that the Commander of the Faithful forbade leading in prayer from a mushaf, and the principle that a prohibition implies invalidity. He responds to the Dhakwan narration by interpreting it as a review before the prayer.
The text says: 'استدلّ لأبي حنيفة له بما روى أبو داود عن ابن عباس ... وأجيب عن أثر ذكوان ... أنه كان مراجعة قبل الصلاة'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the prayer is invalid and must be repeated; under the Sahibayn, it is valid but disliked. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's.
This affects whether a person needs to repeat the prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقراءته من مصحف (۱) [١] قوله : وقراءته ؛ أي المصلّي من مصحف، وهو ما كتب فيه القرآن، سواءً كان إماماً أو مقتدياً، ووجه كونه مفسداً : أنّ الأخذ من المصحفِ تَلَقَّن من الخارج، فتفسد به الصلاة، سواء كان المصحف محمولاً أو موضوعاً، وسواء قَلْبَ المُصلِّي أوراقاً أو قلبها غيره، وهذا هو الصحيح كما في «الكافي» وغيره. وهذا عنده، وعندهما القراءة من المصحف غير مفسدة، بل مكروهة، ومن هاهنا يُعلم أنه لو فتح المقتدي إمامه آخذاً عن المصحف تفسد صلاته وصلاة الإمام أيضاً إن أخذ فتحه، واستدلّ لهما بما روى أن ذكوان مولى عائشة رضي الله عنها «يؤمّ بها في رمضان وكان يقرأ من المصحف (۳). وجه للفساد، وأيضاً النظر في المصحفِ عبادة، فلا يضر ضمّه مع القراءة فلا وإنما يكره هذا الفعل للتشبه بأهل الكتاب، فإنّهم يفعلون كذلك. فإن قلت : لو كره هذا لكره جميع الأفعال المشتركة بيننا وبينهم. قلت : لا ؛ إنما يكره التشبه في أفعالهم المخصوصة بهم، مما لنا منه بد ، ولهذا كره السدل في الصلاة ونحوه. واستدلّ لأبي حنيفة له بما روى أبو داود عن ابن عباس قال : «نهانا أمير المؤمنين أن نؤم الناس في المصحف (۳) ، فإنّ الأصل أن النهي يقتضي الفساد. وأجيب عن أثر ذكوان له من وجوه : أحدهما : ما ذكره الزَّيْلَعِي في شرح الكنز (4) أنه كان مراجعة قبل الصلاة.
And his recitation from a mushaf (1) [1] His statement: 'And his recitation' i.e., the praying person from a mushaf, which is something in which the Quran is written, whether he is an imam or a follower. And the reason for it being invalidating: that taking from the mushaf is learning from an external source, so the prayer is invalidated by it, whether the mushaf is held or placed, and whether the praying person turned pages or someone else turned them. This is the correct view as in al-Kafi and others. This is according to him (Abu Hanifa). According to them (the two disciples), reading from a mushaf is not invalidating, but rather disliked. From here it is known that if a follower prompts his imam by taking from a mushaf, his prayer is invalidated, and the imam's prayer is also invalidated if he took the prompting. And they (the disciples) argued by what was narrated that Dhakwan, the freed slave of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her), used to lead her in prayer in Ramadan and would read from the mushaf. The reason (for non-invalidation): Additionally, looking into the mushaf is an act of worship, so its inclusion with recitation does not harm. This act is only disliked because of imitation of the People of the Book, for they do so. If you say: If this is disliked, then all actions common between us and them would be disliked. I say: No; rather, imitation is disliked in their actions that are specific to them, from which we have an alternative. For this reason, letting the garment hang loose (sadl) in prayer and the like is disliked. And he (Abu Hanifa) argued by what Abu Dawud narrated from Ibn Abbas: 'The Commander of the Faithful forbade us from leading people in prayer while [reading from] a mushaf.' And the principle is that a prohibition implies invalidity. And the narration of Dhakwan was responded to in several ways: One of them: What al-Zayla'i mentioned in Sharh al-Kanz: that it was a review before the prayer.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on reciting from a physical copy of the Quran (mushaf) during prayer. It clarifies that the 'reading from a mushaf' refers to any praying person, whether imam or follower. According to Abu Hanifa, this action invalidates the prayer because it involves learning (talaqun) from an external source, regardless of whether the mushaf is held or placed, and whether the person turns pages himself or someone else does. The correct view in the school, as stated in al-Kafi and others, is this position. According to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), reading from a mushaf does not invalidate the prayer but is disliked (makruh). They base their opinion on the practice of Dhakwan, the freed slave of Aisha, who led her in Ramadan while reading from a mushaf. They argue that looking at the mushaf is an act of worship, so it does not harm the prayer. The only reason it is disliked is to avoid imitating the People of the Book. A potential objection that all common acts with People of the Book would then be disliked is refuted: only actions specific to them and for which we have an alternative are disliked. Abu Hanifa's evidence is the hadith from Ibn Abbas that the Commander of the Faithful (Umar or Ali) forbade leading people in prayer while reading from a mushaf, and the principle that a prohibition implies invalidity. The narration of Dhakwan is interpreted as being a review before the prayer, not during it.

Key Terms
المصحف (al-muṣḥaf): A physical copy of the Quran, whether held in the hand or placed on something.
القراءة من المصحف (al-qirāʾa min al-muṣḥaf): Reciting from a written copy of the Quran during the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling if one recites from a physical copy of the Quran (mushaf) during prayer?
A) The prayer is invalidated.
B) The prayer is disliked but valid.
C) The prayer is perfectly valid.
D) The prayer is invalid only if the mushaf is held in the hand.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon position is that reciting from a mushaf during prayer invalidates it, due to the prohibition hadith and the principle that prohibition implies invalidity.

Reading from Mushaf During Prayer: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on reading from Mushaf in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on reading from a Mushaf during prayer, compare the proofs of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the mufta bihi ruling to determine when prayer must be repeated.

Core Idea
Reading from a Mushaf during prayer invalidates according to Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn deem it merely disliked.

This lesson delves into a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: if a person reads the Quran from a physical copy (Mushaf) while in prayer, does the prayer become invalid? Abu Hanifa holds that it does, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), consider it makruh tanzihi but not invalidating. Understanding the evidence each side uses and why the school ultimately adopts Abu Hanifa's view will sharpen your ability to evaluate legal arguments and apply the relied-upon position in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is reading from a Mushaf during prayer, whether by imam or follower. The dispute centers on whether this act invalidates the prayer (Abu Hanifa) or is only disliked (Sahibayn).
The ruling applies regardless of whether the Mushaf is held or placed, and whether the person turns pages or someone else does.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa's evidence: the hadith of Ibn Abbas that 'Umar forbade leading prayer from a Mushaf, and the usul principle that a prohibition indicates invalidation (fasād).
The author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah declares this view 'al-sahih' (correct).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn cite the hadith of Dhakwan, who led 'Aisha in prayer while reading from a Mushaf. They also argue that looking at the Mushaf is worship, so adding it does not harm; the dislike is due to resemblance to the People of the Book.
They reconcile the prohibition as makruh tanzihi, not invalidation.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Sahibayn's proof
The commentator answers the Dhakwan hadith in two ways: (a) Dhakwan reviewed the Mushaf before prayer, or (b) he had memorized the portion and the narrator mistakenly thought he was reading from it. Thus, the narration does not contradict the general prohibition.
The author also notes that 'Aisha would not approve of a disliked act in her prayer.
5
Determine the practical consequence and the mufta bihi
The thamarah al-khilaf: if one prays reading from a Mushaf, according to Abu Hanifa the prayer is invalid and must be repeated; according to the Sahibayn it is valid but makruh. The mufta bihi position is Abu Hanifa's: the prayer is invalid.
This is the relied-upon view for issuing fatwa, as affirmed by the author.

Source Text

وقراءته من مصحف (۱) عليه رواية أبي داود التي مر ذكرها. [١] قوله : وقراءته ؛ أي المصلّي من مصحف، وهو ما كتب فيه القرآن، سواءً كان إماماً أو مقتدياً، ووجه كونه مفسداً : أنّ الأخذ من المصحفِ تَلَقَّن من الخارج، فتفسد به الصلاة، سواء كان المصحف محمولاً أو موضوعاً، وسواء قَلْبَ المُصلِّي أوراقاً أو قلبها غيره، وهذا هو الصحيح كما في «الكافي» وغيره. وهذا عنده، وعندهما القراءة من المصحف غير مفسدة، بل مكروهة، ومن هاهنا يُعلم أنه لو فتح المقتدي إمامه آخذاً عن المصحف تفسد صلاته وصلاة الإمام أيضاً إن أخذ فتحه، واستدلّ لهما بما روى أن ذكوان مولى عائشة رضي الله عنها «يؤمّ بها في رمضان وكان يقرأ من المصحف (۳). وجه للفساد، وأيضاً النظر في المصحفِ عبادة، فلا يضر ضمّه مع القراءة فلا وإنما يكره هذا الفعل للتشبه بأهل الكتاب، فإنّهم يفعلون كذلك. فإن قلت : لو كره هذا لكره جميع الأفعال المشتركة بيننا وبينهم. قلت : لا ؛ إنما يكره التشبه في أفعالهم المخصوصة بهم، مما لنا منه بد ، ولهذا كره السدل في الصلاة ونحوه. واستدلّ لأبي حنيفة له بما روى أبو داود عن ابن عباس قال : «نهانا أمير المؤمنين أن نؤم الناس في المصحف (۳) ، فإنّ الأصل أن النهي يقتضي الفساد. وأجيب عن أثر ذكوان له من وجوه : أحدهما : ما ذكره الزَّيْلَعِي في شرح الكنز (4) أنه كان مراجعة قبل الصلاة. موضوعاً وسواء (۱) لأن الأخذ المصحف تلقن من الخارج فتفسد به الصلاة سواء كان المصحف محمولاً أو من قلب المصلي أوراقه أو قلبها غيره هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وأما عند أبي يوسف ومحمد فلا تفسد، بل تكره، وعند الشافعي لا يكره، ينظر: «الوسيط (٢ : ١٨٤) ، حلية العلماء» (۲ : ۸۹ وتمام مسائل الأخذ من المصحف في القول الأشرف في الفتح عن المصحف»(ص٥٥). (۲) في «مصنف ابن أبي شيبة (٢ : ٤٢٠). (۳) في «كنز العمال (۲۲۸۳۷) ونسبه إلى ابن أبي داود. (٤) تبيين الحقائق (۱) : ١٥٩). وثانيهما : ما ذكره العيني) وغيره أنه كان يحفظ في كل شفع مقدار ما يقرأ في الركعتين، فظنّ الراوي أنه كان يقرأ من المصحف، ويؤيد ما ذكرنا أن القراءة عن المصحف مكروهة، ولا تظنّ بعائشة رضي الله عنها أنها كانت ترضى بالمكروه وتصلي خلف من يصلّي بصلاة مكروهة، وفي المقام أبحاث ذكرتها في رسالتي : «القول الأشرف في الفتح عن المصحف».
And his reading from a Mushaf (1) upon it is the narration of Abu Dawud which has been mentioned. [1] His saying: 'and his reading' means the one praying from a Mushaf, which is that in which the Quran is written, whether he is an imam or a follower. The reason it invalidates is that taking from the Mushaf is learning from outside, so the prayer is invalidated by it, whether the Mushaf is carried or placed, and whether the person praying turns the pages or someone else turns them. This is the correct view as in 'al-Kafi' and others. This is his view, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) reading from the Mushaf is not invalidating, but rather disliked. From here it is known that if a follower opens a Mushaf and follows it, his prayer and also the imam's prayer become invalid if the imam follows his opening. They argued with what was narrated that Dhakwan, the freed slave of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her), used to lead her in prayer in Ramadan and would read from the Mushaf (3). The aspect of invalidation? Also, looking at the Mushaf is worship, so adding it to recitation does not harm. Rather, this act is only disliked because it resembles the People of the Book, for they do likewise. If you say: If this is disliked, then all acts common between us and them would be disliked. I say: No; only resemblance in their specific acts that we can avoid is disliked. For this reason, letting the garment hang (sidl) in prayer and the like are disliked. He argued for Abu Hanifa with what Abu Dawud narrated from Ibn Abbas: 'The Commander of the Faithful forbade us from leading people in prayer (using) a Mushaf' (3), and the principle is that prohibition implies invalidation. The narration of Dhakwan was answered in several ways: First, what al-Zayla'i mentioned in Sharh al-Kanz (4) that he reviewed it before prayer. (As for) placed or... (1) Because taking from the Mushaf is learning from outside, so the prayer is invalidated by it whether the Mushaf is carried or the one praying turns its pages or someone else turns them. This according to Abu Hanifa. As for Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, it does not invalidate but is disliked. According to al-Shafi'i, it is not disliked. See: 'al-Wasit' (2:184), 'Hilyat al-Ulama' (2:89) and the complete issues of taking from the Mushaf are in 'al-Qawl al-Ashraf fi al-Fath 'an al-Mushaf' (p. 55). (2) In 'Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba' (2:420). (3) In 'Kanz al-Ummal' (22837) attributed to Ibn Abi Dawud. (4) 'Tabyin al-Haqa'iq' (1:159). Secondly, what al-Ayni and others mentioned: that he used to memorize in every shaf' (pair of cycles) the amount he would recite in the two rak'ahs, so the narrator thought he was reading from the Mushaf. This supports what we said: that reading from the Mushaf is disliked, and it is not to be thought that Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) would be pleased with a disliked act and pray behind someone who prays with a disliked prayer. In this context, there are investigations that I have mentioned in my treatise: 'al-Qawl al-Ashraf fi al-Fath 'an al-Mushaf'.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding reading from the Mushaf during prayer. The matn states that reading from a Mushaf invalidates the prayer. The commentator explains that this refers to any person praying, whether imam or follower, reading from a written copy of the Quran. The reason given is that taking recitation from an external source (the Mushaf) is akin to learning from outside the prayer, which introduces an extraneous action that invalidates the prayer. This is Abu Hanifa's position. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), reading from a Mushaf does not invalidate but is merely disliked (makruh). The commentator clarifies that if a follower opens a Mushaf and recites from it, his prayer becomes invalid, and if the imam follows that recitation, his prayer also becomes invalid. The Sahibayn's proof is the hadith of Dhakwan, who used to lead Aisha in Ramadan while reading from a Mushaf, indicating that the act is permissible. They also argue that looking at the Mushaf is an act of worship, so combining it with recitation does not harm; the dislike is only due to resemblance to the People of the Book. The commentator refutes this by explaining that resemblance to specific acts of the People of the Book that we can avoid is disliked, not all common acts. Abu Hanifa's proof is the hadith of Ibn Abbas that Umar forbade leading prayer from a Mushaf, and the principle that a prohibition indicates invalidation. The narration of Dhakwan is interpreted in two ways: either he reviewed the Mushaf before the prayer, or he had memorized the portion he would recite, and the observer mistakenly thought he was reading from the Mushaf. The commentator wrote a separate treatise on this issue, emphasizing that the relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's, as stated in 'al-Kafi' and other works.

Key Terms
مصحف (mushaf): A physical copy of the Quran written in pages, as opposed to recitation from memory.
تشبه بأهل الكتاب (tashabbuh bi-ahl al-kitab): Imitating the People of the Book in their practices, which is disliked in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi mufta bihi, what is the ruling if a person reads from a Mushaf during prayer?
A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer is valid but makruh.
C) The prayer is valid and not makruh.
D) The prayer becomes invalid only if the Mushaf is held in the hand.

Key Takeaway
Reading from a Mushaf during prayer invalidates the prayer according to the relied-upon Hanafi position; the Sahibayn's view that it is only disliked is a minority opinion within the school.

Three Invalidators of Prayer: Prostration on Impurity, Worldly Du'a, and Eating/Drinking
Commentary on three invalidators of prayer: prostration on impurity, worldly du'a, and eating/drinking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three invalidators of prayer according to the Hanafi school and explain the conditions under which each invalidates.

Core Idea
Prayer is invalidated by prostration on impurity, worldly du'a before tashahhud, and any eating or drinking.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains three key invalidators (mufsidat) of prayer. Understanding these helps ensure the validity of salah and avoid common pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three invalidators
Read the passage and list the three acts that invalidate prayer: prostration on impurity, worldly supplication, and eating/drinking.
These are explicitly mentioned in the source.
2
Analyze the first invalidator: prostration on impurity
Note that the place of prostration must be pure. There is an ikhtilaf on whether hands and knees also require purity; the relied-upon view is yes.
The source cites 'al-Munya' and 'Nur al-Idah' for the operative view.
3
Analyze the second invalidator: worldly du'a
Du'a that resembles human speech (e.g., asking for a specific spouse or money) invalidates only if it occurs before the tashahhud. After tashahhud, it does not invalidate.
The source distinguishes between Quranic/hadith du'a (valid) and worldly requests.
4
Analyze the third invalidator: eating and drinking
Any eating or drinking, whether intentional or forgetful, invalidates prayer because it is a lot of action and the state of prayer requires mindfulness.
Swallowing something stuck between teeth does not invalidate.

كتاب الصلاة ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها وسجوده على نجس ، والدُّعاء بما يسألُ من النَّاسِ (۳) ، نحو: اللهم زوجني فلانة ، أو أعطنى ألف دينار، ونحو ذلك ، وأكله ، وشربه ) (1)4 [٤] [١]قوله : وسجوده على نجس ؛ أي موضع نجس ، فهو بكسر الجيم، فإذا سجد على نجس فسدت صلاته، وإن أعاد السجود على طاهر ، وهذا لاشتراط طهارة مكان السجدة لصحة الصلاة باتفاق أصحابنا على المذهب المعتبر. وهل تشترط طهارة موضع الركبتين واليدين أم لا ؟ اختلف أصحابنا فيه، والمعمول الاشتراط كما في «المنية وشرحها ، ونور الإيضاح» وغيره، وقد بسطنا الكلام في هذا المقام في «السعاية» في شرح «باب شروط الصلاة». [٢]قوله : والدعاء ؛ قال في «السراج الوهاج»: «الـذي يشبه كلام الناس إنمـا يفسد إذا كان قبل تمام فرائضها ، أما إذا كان بعد التشهد فلا يفسدها». انتهى. وفي «العناية»: حقيقة الكلام بعد التشهد غير مفسد فكيف بما يشبه، وهذا عندهما ظاهر، وكذا عند أبي حنيفة الله ؛ لأنَّ ما يشبه كلام الناس صنع منه ، صلاته به ، فكان بالدعاء الذي يشبه كلام الناس خارجاً منها لا مفسداً». انتهى. [٣]قوله : بما يسأل من الناس ؛ مجهول أو معروف ، قال الحلبي في «شرح المنية» : «إنّ ما هو في القرآن والحديث لا يفسد، وما ليس في أحدهما إن استحال طلبه من الخلق لا يفسد ، وإلا يفسد لو قبل قدر التشهد، وإلا تتم به». [٤]قوله : وأكله وشربه ؛ سواء كان عامداً أو ناسيا ؛ لأنه عمل كثير، وحالة الصلاة مذكرة ، فلا يعفى فيها النسيان، ولو ابتلع شيئاً بين أسنانه لا تفسد صلاته ؛ لأنه
Book of Prayer: What Invalidates Prayer and What is Disliked Therein. And his prostration on impurity, and the supplication asking from people (3), such as: O Allah, marry me to so-and-so, or give me a thousand dinars, and the like, and his eating and drinking. ) (1)4 [4] [1] His saying: 'And his prostration on impurity' means a place of impurity, so it is with kasr of the jim. So if he prostrates on impurity, his prayer becomes invalid, even if he repeats the prostration on a pure place. This is because purity of the place of prostration is a condition for the validity of prayer by agreement of our companions on the relied-upon school. And whether purity of the place of the knees and hands is required or not? Our companions differed on it, and the operative view is the requirement as in 'al-Munya' and its commentary, 'Nur al-Idah', and others. We have expanded the discussion on this point in 'al-Si'aya' in the commentary on 'Bab Shurut al-Salah'. [2] His saying: 'and the supplication'; he said in 'al-Siraj al-Wahhaj': 'That which resembles human speech only invalidates if it occurs before the completion of the obligatory acts (fara'id), but if after the tashahhud, it does not invalidate.' End quote. And in 'al-Inaya': 'The reality of speech after tashahhud does not invalidate, so how about that which resembles it? This is apparent according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and likewise according to Abu Hanifa (May Allah be pleased with him), because that which resembles human speech is an act from him, his prayer is completed by it, so through the supplication that resembles human speech, he exits from it, not that it invalidates.' End quote. [3] His saying: 'what he asks from people' – whether unknown or known. Al-Halabi said in 'Sharh al-Munya': 'What is in the Quran and hadith does not invalidate. What is not in either of them: if it is impossible to request from created beings, it does not invalidate; otherwise, it invalidates if before the amount of tashahhud, otherwise it completes it.' [4] His saying: 'And his eating and drinking' – whether intentionally or forgetfully, because it is a lot of action, and the state of prayer is one of remembrance, so forgetfulness is not excused in it. And if he swallows something between his teeth, his prayer does not invalidate, because...
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage addresses three invalidators of prayer (mufsidat) as part of the book on prayer. The first is prostration on a najis (impure) place. If the place of prostration is impure, the prayer becomes invalid even if one repeats the prostration on a clean spot. The condition of purity for the place of prostration is agreed upon by the Hanafis. However, there is a difference of opinion whether the place for the knees and hands must also be pure; the relied-upon view (al-mu'tamad) is that it is required, as stated in reliable texts like 'al-Munya' and 'Nur al-Idah'. The second invalidator is supplication (du'a) that resembles human speech, i.e., worldly requests like asking for marriage to a specific person or wealth. According to 'al-Siraj al-Wahhaj', such du'a invalidates only if done before the completion of the obligatory acts of prayer (i.e., before the final tashahhud). After tashahhud, normal speech does not invalidate, so du'a that resembles speech also does not invalidate. The commentator from 'al-Inaya' explains that this is clear according to the Sahibayn and also Abu Hanifa: such du'a is considered an act of prayer completion, not invalidation. The third invalidator is eating and drinking. Any consumption, whether intentional or forgetful, invalidates the prayer because it is a lot of action and the state of prayer requires mindfulness. However, swallowing something stuck between one's teeth (e.g., a food remnant) does not invalidate, as it is not considered intentional eating.

Key Terms
نجس (najis): Impure substance or place; here specifically the place of prostration being impure.
الدعاء (al-du'a): Supplication; here specifically worldly requests that resemble human speech.
أكل وشرب (akl wa shurb): Eating and drinking; invalidates prayer whether intentional or forgetful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does eating or drinking during prayer invalidate it if done forgetfully?
A) No, forgetfulness is excused.
B) Yes, it invalidates even if forgetful.
C) Only if the amount is large.
D) It is disliked but does not invalidate.

Key Takeaway
Three clear invalidators: impurity on sajda place, worldly du'a before tashahhud, and any eating/drinking — each has its own condition.

Prostration on Impurity and the Requirement of Purity for Knees and Hands
Ruling on prostration on impurity with intra-Hanafi dispute on limbs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on prostration on impurity and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether purity of the knees and hands is required, identifying the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
If the place of prostration is impure, prayer is invalid; the relied-upon Hanafi position extends this to knees and hands.

Prostration on an impure place invalidates prayer because purity of the place of prostration is a condition. But what about the knees and hands? Hanafi scholars differed, and the relied-upon position requires purity there as well. This lesson examines the ruling and the intra-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on prostration on impurity
The passage states that if one prostrates on an impure spot, prayer is invalidated (fasid), even if repeated on pure ground.
2
Understand the basis: condition of purity of place
Purity of the place of prostration (طهارة مكان السجدة) is a condition for validity, agreed upon by Hanafis.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi scholars questioned whether the condition extends to the place of knees and hands.
4
Determine the relied-upon position
The operative opinion (المعمول) is that it is required, as stated in al-Munya, Nur al-Idah, etc.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If not required, praying with impure knees/hands would be valid; but according to the mufta bihi, it is not.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسجوده على نجس. قوله : وسجوده على نجس؛ أي موضع نجس ، فهو بكسر الجيم، فإذا سجد على نجس فسدت صلاته، وإن أعاد السجود على طاهر ، وهذا لاشتراط طهارة مكان السجدة لصحة الصلاة باتفاق أصحابنا على المذهب المعتبر. وهل تشترط طهارة موضع الركبتين واليدين أم لا ؟ اختلف أصحابنا فيه، والمعمول الاشتراط كما في «المنية وشرحها ، ونور الإيضاح» وغيره، وقد بسطنا الكلام في هذا المقام في «السعاية» في شرح «باب شروط الصلاة».
His prostration on impurity. He said: 'His prostration on impurity' means a place of impurity, so it is with kasra on the jim. When he prostrates on impurity, his prayer is invalidated, even if he repeats the prostration on clean ground. This is because purity of the place of prostration is a condition for the validity of prayer by agreement of our companions according to the relied-upon school. And is purity of the place of knees and hands required or not? Our companions differed on it, and the operative opinion is the requirement, as stated in 'al-Munya' and its commentary, and 'Nur al-Idah', and others. And we have expanded the discussion in this matter in 'al-Si'aya' in the commentary on 'Chapter of Conditions of Prayer'.

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific invalidator of prayer: prostration on a place of najasah. The ruling is clear: if one prostrates on a spot that is impure, the prayer is invalidated (fasid), even if one subsequently repeats the prostration on a pure spot. The reasoning given is that the purity of the place of prostration (طهارة مكان السجدة) is a condition for the validity of prayer, and this is agreed upon by the Hanafis on the relied-upon position. However, there is an internal disagreement (ikhtilaf) among Hanafi scholars about whether purity is required for the place of the knees and hands as well. The text states that the operative position (المعمول) is that it is required, citing sources like 'al-Munya' and 'Nur al-Idah'. The author mentions having expanded on this in his own work 'al-Si'aya'. Thus, this lesson is primarily about an intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of the condition of purity for the limbs in prostration.

Key Terms
نجس (najas): Impurity (physical filth), here meaning the place of prostration is contaminated by an impure substance.
سجود (sujud): Prostration, a pillar of prayer. The passage discusses the place of sujud (masjid).
طهارة مكان السجدة (taharat makan al-sajdah): Purity of the place of prostration; a condition for the validity of prayer.

Key Takeaway
Prostration on impurity invalidates prayer, and the relied-upon Hanafi view extends this condition to the place of the knees and hands.

Supplication Resembling Human Talk in Prayer
Ruling on du'a that resembles human talk in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on invalidation of prayer due to supplication resembling human talk and apply the temporal condition (before/after tashahhud) to determine validity.

Core Idea
Supplication that resembles asking people invalidates prayer only if before the final tashahhud.

In prayer, we are permitted to make du'a, but not all supplications are equal. The Hanafi school distinguishes between du'a that is clearly worship and du'a that sounds like a request from a human being. This lesson explains exactly when such requests invalidate the prayer and when they do not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of du'a
Determine whether the supplication is from the Quran or authentic hadith, or if it is a request for something typically asked from people. If from Quran/hadith, it never invalidates.
Du'a like 'O Allah, give me Jannah' is not like human talk because it cannot be obtained from people.
2
Locate the timing in prayer
Check whether the du'a occurs before completing the obligatory parts (i.e., before the final tashahhud) or after.
After tashahhud, speech does not invalidate; the prayer is essentially complete.
3
Apply the ruling
If the du'a resembles human talk and occurs before tashahhud, the prayer is invalid. If after tashahhud, the prayer remains valid.
The prayer is invalid (batil) and must be repeated from the beginning.
4
Check the exception for impossible requests
If the du'a is not from Quran/hadith but is a request that cannot possibly be fulfilled by a human (e.g., 'O Allah, give me paradise'), it does not invalidate regardless of timing.
This exception shows the principle: only du'a that mimics human conversation (i.e., could be said to another person) invalidates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدعاء بما يسأل من الناس. قال في «السراج الوهاج»: «الذي يشبه كلام الناس إنما يفسد إذا كان قبل تمام فرائضها، أما إذا كان بعد التشهد فلا يفسدها». انتهى. وفي «العناية»: حقيقة الكلام بعد التشهد غير مفسد فكيف بما يشبه، وهذا عندهما ظاهر، وكذا عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأنَّ ما يشبه كلام الناس صنع منه صلاته به، فكان بالدعاء الذي يشبه كلام الناس خارجاً منها لا مفسداً. انتهى. قوله : بما يسأل من الناس؛ مجهول أو معروف، قال الحلبي في «شرح المنية»: «إنّ ما هو في القرآن والحديث لا يفسد، وما ليس في أحدهما إن استحال طلبه من الخلق لا يفسد، وإلا يفسد لو قبل قدر التشهد، وإلا تتم به».
And the supplication asking from people. He said in 'al-Siraj al-Wahhaj': 'That which resembles human speech only invalidates if it is before the completion of the obligatory parts (of prayer). As for after the tashahhud, it does not invalidate.' End. And in 'al-'Inaya': 'The essence of speech after tashahhud is not invalidating, so how about what resembles it? This is apparent according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and likewise according to Abu Hanifa, because that which resembles human speech is something constructed from his prayer? So by the supplication that resembles human speech, he exits from the prayer, but it does not invalidate.' End. He said: 'Asking from people' — whether unknown or known. Al-Halabi said in 'Sharh al-Munya': 'Whatever is in the Quran or hadith does not invalidate. What is not in either: if it is impossible to request from creation, it does not invalidate; otherwise, it invalidates if before the extent of tashahhud, and if after, it completes it.'
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage discusses a form of du'a that resembles human speech (e.g., asking for marriage or money from people). The ruling is that such supplication invalidates the prayer if it occurs before completing the obligatory parts (i.e., before the final tashahhud). However, if after the tashahhud, it does not invalidate. The text quotes from 'al-Siraj al-Wahhaj' and 'al-'Inaya', noting that Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree on this. Further, al-Halabi adds that if the du'a is from the Quran or authenticated hadith, it does not invalidate. If not, then if the request is something that cannot be obtained from people (e.g., 'O Allah, give me Jannah'), it also does not invalidate. Otherwise, it invalidates before the tashahhud. The key point is that supplication that is essentially a request from people is considered like human talk and has the same ruling as speaking.

Key Terms
دعاء (du'a): Supplication, specifically here referring to asking for worldly things from people in a manner resembling human conversation.
تشهد (tashahhud): The final sitting in prayer where the testimony of faith is recited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person in prayer says, 'O Allah, marry me to Fatima,' before the tashahhud, what is the ruling?
A) Prayer is invalid because it resembles human talk.
B) Prayer is valid because it is a du'a.
C) Prayer is invalid only if Fatima is present.
D) Prayer is valid because marriage is a religious matter.

Key Takeaway
Du'a that resembles asking people invalidates prayer before tashahhud; after tashahhud it does not; du'a from Quran/hadith or impossible requests never invalidates.

Eating and Drinking Invalidates Prayer: Forgetfulness and Incidental Particles
Ruling on eating/drinking and the exception for particles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that eating or drinking during prayer invalidates it, even if done forgetfully, and identify the exception for swallowing incidental particles like a small piece of food stuck between teeth.

Core Idea
Eating or drinking invalidates prayer—even forgetfully—unless it is an incidental particle between teeth.

We now examine a clear ruling from the Hanafi school: eating or drinking during the prayer invalidates it, regardless of intention or forgetfulness. The reasoning is that such actions constitute 'amal katheer (excessive action) and are fundamentally incompatible with the state of prayer. However, an exception exists for swallowing tiny, incidental particles that one cannot avoid, such as a morsel stuck between the teeth. This ruling also mirrors the law of fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage clearly states that eating and drinking during prayer invalidate it. Note that no distinction is made between intentional and forgetful acts.
The ruling is absolute: any eating or drinking, except the special case below.
2
Understand why forgetfulness is not excused
The author provides the rationale: the state of prayer is a constant reminder (mudhakkir). Therefore, forgetfulness does not excuse an act that negates the essence of prayer.
This is a key Hanafi principle: prayer's sanctity overrides simple forgetfulness.
3
Recognize the exception for incidental particles
If one swallows something that was already between the teeth (e.g., a tiny food particle), the prayer remains valid because the action is incidental (taba') and not deliberate eating.
The example given is the size of a chickpea; larger amounts invalidate prayer.
4
Apply the analogous ruling to fasting
The same logic applies to fasting: swallowing a small incidental particle does not break the fast, but a large amount does. This shows consistency across ibadat.
The text explicitly cites this analogy from 'al-Dhakhira'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأكله وشربه. سواء كان عامداً أو ناسيا؛ لأنه عمل كثير، وحالة الصلاة مذكرة، فلا يعفى فيها النسيان، ولو ابتلع شيئاً بين أسنانه لا تفسد صلاته؛ لأنه تبع، ولهذا لا يفسد الصوم به إذا كان قليلاً كالحمصة، فإن كان كثيراً تفسد. كذا في «الذخيرة».
And his eating and drinking. Whether intentionally or forgetfully; because it is excessive action, and the state of prayer is a reminder, so forgetfulness is not excused in it. If he swallows something between his teeth, his prayer does not become invalid because it is incidental. For this reason, fasting is not invalidated by it if it is small like a chickpea, but if it is much, it invalidates. This is in 'al-Dhakhira'.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling: eating or drinking during prayer invalidates it, regardless of whether the person did it intentionally or forgetfully. The reason given is that eating/drinking is considered 'amal katheer (excessive action), and since prayer is a state that continually reminds the person of its sanctity, forgetfulness is not excused. However, there is an exception: if one swallows a small particle stuck between the teeth (like a piece of food), it does not invalidate the prayer because it is incidental (taba'). The same principle applies to fasting: if the particle is small like a chickpea, fasting is not invalidated; but if it is large, it invalidates. This ruling is cited from 'al-Dhakhira'.

Key Terms
عمل كثير (amal katheer): Excessive action; eating and drinking are considered 'amal katheer even if small in quantity, because they are unrelated to prayer.
نسيان (nisyan): Forgetfulness; in prayer, forgetfulness does not excuse eating/drinking because the state of prayer is a reminder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) No, because it is incidental and small.
C) Yes, if done intentionally.
D) No, only if it is larger than a chickpea.

Key Takeaway
Eating or drinking during prayer invalidates it even if forgetful, with the sole exception of swallowing an incidental small particle stuck between the teeth.
