Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Excessive Action (Amal Katheer)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on defining excessive action in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five Hanafi opinions on 'amal katheer and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Excessive action invalidates prayer, but its definition is disputed among Hanafis—ranging from two hands to three movements to intention.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary explores the intra-Hanafi disagreement on what exactly constitutes 'amal katheer (excessive action) that invalidates prayer. While all Hanafis agree that actions incompatible with prayer break it, they differ on the criterion for 'excessive'. The commentator presents multiple opinions, refuting a deviant view, and the mufta bihi is clarified. Understanding this dispute is essential for a nuanced application of prayer rulings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the General Principle
The passage first states that excessive action invalidates prayer due to incompatibility, but actions of prayer itself (extra ruku') or for its rectification do not invalidate.
2
Identify the Five Opinions
List the five definitions: (1) customarily done with both hands, (2) three consecutive movements, (3) intention-based/separate activity, (4) from narration of fanning (three movements), (5) deviant view that raising hands invalidates.
The first three are from the commentator; the fourth and fifth are added.
3
Examine the Evidence for Each
For the three-movement opinion, the narration from Abu Hanifa about fanning; for intention, the case of passionate touch; for both hands, custom.
4
Identify the Rejected Opinion
The opinion that raising hands at ruku' and sujud invalidates is rejected as shadhdh (deviant) in authoritative works.
Referenced in Fath al-Qadir, al-Halba, al-Bazzaziyya.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The mufta bihi is the first opinion (customary use of both hands), and raising hands does not invalidate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل عمل كثير. قوله : وكل عمل كثير؛ هو مقيد بما لم يكن من أعمال الصلاة، ولا لإصلاحها، فلو زاد ركوعاً أو سجوداً لا تفسد، والوضوء والمشي عند سبق الحدث أيضا لا يفسد، وإن كان كل ذلك عملاً كثيراً، والوجه في فساد الصلاة بالعمل الكثير أن بينهما منافاة، فبورود المنافي يبطل المنافي. قوله : اختلف؛ ذكر العيني وغيره أن فيه أقوالاً خمسة، ذكر الشارح له منها هاهنا ثلاثاً. والرابع: أن الحركات الثلاث المتوالية كثير وما دونها قليل، واستندوا في ذلك بما روى عن أبي حنيفة أنَّ المصلي إن روح بمروحة مرتين لا تفسد، وبثلاث تفسد. والخامس: أن ما يكون مقصوداً للفاعل بأن يفرد له مجلساً على حدة كثير، وما سواه قليل؛ ولهذا قالوا: لو مس من المصليّة زوجها أو قبلها بشهوة أو مص ثديها صبي وخرج منها اللبن فسدت صلاتها. قوله : فقيل؛ هو ما يحتاج حاصله: أن ما يعمل باليدين عادة كثير، وإن عمل بواحدة كالتعمّم وشدّ السراويل، وما عمل بواحدة قليل، وإن عمل بهما كحل السراويل ولُبس القلنسوة إلا إذا تكرر ثلاث مرات متوالية، ويتفرع على هذا القول ما ذكر في بعض الكتب أن الصلاة تفسد برفع اليدين عند الركوع وعند السجود، وهو قول شاد مردود، كما في «فتح القدير» و«الحلبة» و«البَزَّازية» وغيرها، وقد بسطت الكلام فيه في «الفوائد البهية في تراجم الحنفية عند ذكر ترجمة مكحول النسفي الحنفي».
And every excessive action. He said: 'And every excessive action' — it is restricted to what is not from the actions of prayer nor for its rectification. So if he adds an extra bowing or prostration, it does not invalidate. And performing ablution and walking due to preceding hadath also does not invalidate, even if all of that is excessive action. The reason for invalidation of prayer by excessive action is that there is incompatibility between them, so when an opposite comes, it nullifies the opposite. He said: 'They differed' — al-‘Ayni and others mentioned that there are five opinions on it, of which the commentator mentioned three here. The fourth: that three consecutive movements are excessive and what is less is little, and they based that on what was narrated from Abu Hanifa that if the praying person fans himself twice with a fan, it does not invalidate, but with three it invalidates. The fifth: that what is intended by the doer, such that he devotes a separate sitting to it, is excessive; otherwise it is little. For this reason they said: If a husband touches his wife who is praying or kisses her with desire, or a child sucks her breast and milk comes out, her prayer is invalidated. He said: 'It is said' — the summary: that what is customarily done with two hands is excessive, even if done with one like turban-wrapping and tightening trousers, and what is done with one is little, even if done with both like loosening trousers and wearing a cap, unless it is repeated three consecutive times. And derived from this opinion is what is mentioned in some books that prayer is invalidated by raising the hands at bowing and at prostration, and it is a rejected opinion, as in 'Fath al-Qadir', 'al-Halba', 'al-Bazzaziyya', and others. And I have expanded the discussion in 'al-Fawaid al-Bahiyya fi Tarajim al-Hanafiyya' under the biography of Makhul al-Nasafi al-Hanafi.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage deals with the concept of 'amal katheer (excessive action) that invalidates prayer. The commentary first clarifies that not all actions that are 'excessive' invalidate prayer; only those that are not part of the prayer's own actions (like extra ruku’) or for its rectification (like ablution due to hadath). The underlying reason is incompatibility: excessive actions that are foreign to prayer nullify its validity. The main part of the passage discusses the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the exact definition of 'amal katheer. The text mentions that there are five opinions, of which the commentator (the author of 'al-Wiqaya' or its commentary) gave three. The author then adds two more: the fourth opinion defines three consecutive movements as excessive; the fifth defines it based on intention (if the action is done as a separate activity, it is excessive). These are illustrated with examples: fanning thrice, or a husband touching his wife with desire. The text also mentions the opinion that defines excessive action by the use of both hands, and it refutes the deviant opinion that raising hands during ruku' and sujud invalidates prayer. This lesson is a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute on a key concept in prayer invalidation.

Key Terms
عمل كثير (amal katheer): Excessive action that is incompatible with prayer; its definition is disputed among Hanafis.
منافاة (munafah): Incompatibility between excessive action and the state of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One movement.
B) Two movements.
C) Three movements.
D) Four movements.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes multiple definitions of 'amal katheer, but the mufta bihi is the customary use of both hands, and raising hands does not invalidate prayer.

Defining Excessive Action That Invalidates Prayer
Intra-Hanafi dispute defining 'excessive action' in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five Hanafi definitions of al-amal al-kathir and explain why the majority prefer the first two definitions.

Core Idea
Excessive action in prayer is not a fixed number; Hanafi scholars define it by hand movement, observer perception, or worshipper's judgment.

The Hanafi school agrees that excessive extraneous action invalidates salah, but what counts as 'excessive' is a matter of ijtihad. In this passage, the commentator presents five definitions from the early masters, each with practical consequences. Understanding this internal debate sharpens your ability to apply the rule correctly in real prayer scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
The core debate is not whether large amounts of action invalidate prayer—that is settled. Rather, it is about how to define 'al-amal al-kathir' (excessive action).
The commentator lists five sayings, focusing on three.
2
List the five sayings
1) What requires both hands customarily. 2) What an observer (from a distance) would know is not prayer. 3) What the worshipper himself deems much. 4) Three consecutive movements (based on fanning narration). 5) Action intended as a separate act.
The first two are considered most practical.
3
Analyze the majority's tarjih
The majority (al-jumhur) chose the first two sayings because they encompass most furu' and are objective. The second saying is not seen as distinct from the first, since customarily hand movements trigger observer suspicion.
The third saying (worshipper's judgment) is closest to Abu Hanifa's methodology but deemed too subjective.
4
Apply the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon position combines the hand-movement and observer-perception criteria. For fatwa, an action that either requires both hands customarily or would make a distant observer doubt prayer is considered excessive.
This definition excludes minor actions like swatting a fly once or adjusting clothing with one hand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل عمل كثير)، اختلف مشايخنا في تفسير العمل الكثير: فقيل: هو ما يحتاج فيه إلى تحريك اليدين. تبع، ولهذا لا يفسد الصوم به إذا كان قليلاً كالحمصة، فإن كان كثيراً تفسد. كذا في «الذخيرة».

[١] قوله: وكل عمل كثير؛ هو مقيد بما لم يكن من أعمال الصلاة، ولا لإصلاحها، فلو زاد ركوعاً أو سجوداً لا تفسد، والوضوء والمشي عند سبق الحدث أيضا لا يفسد، وإن كان كل ذلك عملاً كثيراً، والوجه في فساد الصلاة بالعمل الكثير أن بينهما منافاة، فبورود المنافي يبطل المنافي.

[٢] قوله: اختلف؛ ذكر العيني وغيره أن فيه أقوالاً خمسة، ذكر الشارح له منها هاهنا ثلاثاً. والرابع: أن الحركات الثلاث المتوالية كثير وما دونها قليل، واستندوا في ذلك بما روي عن أبي حنيفة أن المصلي إن روح بمروحة مرتين لا تفسد، وبثلاث تفسد. والخامس: أن ما يكون مقصوداً للفاعل بأن يفرد له مجلساً على حدة كثير، وما سواه قليل؛ ولهذا قالوا: لو مس من المصليّة زوجها أو قبلها بشهوة أو مص ثديها صبي وخرج منها اللبن فسدت صلاتها.

[٣] قوله: فقيل؛ هو ما يحتاج حاصله: أن ما يعمل باليدين عادة كثير، وإن عمل بواحدة كالتعمّم وشدّ السراويل، وما عمل بواحدة قليل، وإن عمل بهما كحل السراويل ولُبس القلنسوة إلا إذا تكرر ثلاث مرات متوالية، ويتفرع على هذا القول ما ذكر في بعض الكتب أن الصلاة تفسد برفع اليدين عند الركوع وعند السجود، وهو قول شاد مردود، كما في «فتح القدير» و«الحلبة» و«البزازية» وغيرها.

وقيل: ما يعلم ناظره أن عامله غير مصلّ، وعامة المشايخ على هذا. وقيل: ما يستكثره المصلي، قال الإمام السرخسي: هذا أقرب إلى مذهب أبي حنيفة، فإن دأبه التفويض إلى رأي المبتلى به.

[١] قوله: وقيل؛ هذا القول صححه في «البدائع» و«التبيين» و«الولوالجية»، وقال في «المحيط»: إنه الأحسن، وقال الصدر الشهيد إنه الصواب، وفي «الخانية» و«الخلاصة»: إنه اختيار العامة.

[٢] قوله: ما يعلم؛ المراد بالعلم ما يشمل الظن كما أشار إليه الغزي في «تنوير الأبصار»، حيث قال: ما لا يشك الناظر في فاعله أنه ليس فيها، والمراد بالناظر من ليس له علم بشروع المصلي في الصلاة، كما في «البحر» و«الحلبة»، ولذا قيده في «النهر» و«البدائع» بقوله: «من بعيد»، بناء على أن القريب لا يخفى عليه الحال عادة.

[٣] قوله: هذا أقرب... الخ؛ يعني أن مسلك أبي حنيفة في مثل هذا الوضع الذي لم يرد فيه تقدير من جانب الشارع التفويض إلى رأي المبتلى بذلك الأمر وظنه، فهذا التفسير يوافق مسلكه في نظائره.

قال شارح «المنية»: ولكنه غير مضبوط، وتفويض مثله إلى رأي العوام مما لا ينبغي، وأكثر الفروع أو جميعها مفرع على الأولين يعني ما يحتاج فيه إلى اليدين، وما يعلم به الناظر أنه غير مصل.

والظاهر أن القول الثاني يعني المذكور في هذا الشرح أولاً ليس خارجاً عن الأول يعني المذكور هاهنا ثانياً؛ لأن ما يقام باليدين عادة يغلب ظن الناظر أنه ليس في الصلاة، وكذا قول من اعتبر التكرار ثلاثاً؛ فلذا اختاره الجمهور.
And every much action: Our scholars differed in interpreting 'much action': It is said: It is that which requires moving both hands. This follows, and for this reason it does not invalidate fasting if it is little, like a chickpea, but if it is much, it invalidates. Thus in al-Dhakhira. [1] His statement: 'and every much action' is restricted to what is not from the actions of prayer nor for its rectification; so if one adds an extra bow or prostration, it does not invalidate, and wudu and walking due to a preceding hadath also do not invalidate, even if all that is much action. The rationale for the invalidation of prayer by much action is that there is incompatibility between them, so when the contradictory occurs, the contradictory is nullified. [2] His statement: 'they differed'; al-Ayni and others mentioned that there are five sayings on it; the commentator mentioned three of them here. The fourth: that three consecutive movements are much and less than that is little, and they based that on what is narrated from Abu Hanifa that if a worshipper fans himself with a fan twice, it does not invalidate, and with three times it invalidates. The fifth: that what is intended by the doer, such that he sets aside a separate sitting for it, is much, and other than that is little; for this reason they said: if a woman touches her husband or kisses him with desire, or a child sucks her breast and milk comes out, her prayer is invalidated. [3] His statement: 'It is said'; its summary: that what is customarily done with both hands is much, even if done with one hand, like wearing a turban or tightening trousers; and what is done with one hand is little, even if done with both hands like loosening trousers or putting on a cap, unless it is repeated three consecutive times. Branched from this saying is what is mentioned in some books that raising the hands at the time of bowing and prostration invalidates prayer, and it is an odd and rejected statement, as in Fath al-Qadir, al-Hulba, al-Bazzaziyya and others. And it is said: that which an observer knows that its doer is not a prayer. And the generality of the mashayikh are upon this. And it is said: that which the worshipper deems much. Imam al-Sarakhsi said: this is closer to the madhhab of Abu Hanifa, for his practice is to delegate to the opinion of the one tested. [1] His statement: 'And it is said'; this saying was declared correct in al-Badai', al-Tabyin, and al-Walwaljiyya, and in al-Muhit it is said to be the best, and al-Sadr al-Shahid said it is correct, and in al-Khaniyya and al-Khulasa it is said to be the choice of the generality. [2] His statement: 'that which an observer knows'; the meaning of knowledge includes what includes conjecture, as al-Ghazzi indicated in Tanwir al-Absar, where he said: that which the observer does not doubt that its doer is not in it. And the observer is one who does not know of the commencement of the worshipper's prayer, as in al-Bahr and al-Hulba. Hence in al-Nahr and al-Badai' it is restricted by 'from afar', based on the fact that the near one usually does not have the situation hidden. [3] His statement: 'this is closer...' means that the way of Abu Hanifa in such a situation, where no estimation has come from the Lawgiver, is to delegate to the opinion of the one tested with that matter and his conjecture. So this interpretation agrees with his way in its parallels. The commentator of al-Munya said: But it is not well-defined, and delegating the like to the opinion of the common people is not proper. And most or all of the furu' are branched upon the first two meanings: that which requires the hands and that which the observer knows the doer is not praying. And it is apparent that the second saying, meaning that mentioned first in this commentary, is not outside the first, meaning that mentioned second here, because what is customarily done with both hands usually makes the observer strongly think that he is not in prayer. Similarly, the saying that considers three repetitions, so the majority chose it.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the definition of al-amal al-kathir (excessive action) that invalidates salah. The commentator begins by quoting the base text's phrase 'and every much action' and immediately notes that our Hanafi scholars differed in its interpretation. Five sayings are mentioned, but the commentator focuses on three. The first saying defines 'much action' as that which requires moving both hands; a single hand is insufficient for it to be considered much. This is tied to a similar ruling in fasting: a small amount like a chickpea does not break the fast, but a large amount does. The second saying defines it as that which an observer (who does not know the person is in prayer) would conclude that the doer is not praying. The majority of mashayikh adopt this view, and it is specified that the observer must be at a distance. The third saying is that it is what the worshipper himself considers much, which Imam al-Sarakhsi says is closest to Abu Hanifa's methodology of delegating the estimation to the one experiencing it, since no textual measure exists. The commentator also mentions a fourth saying: three consecutive movements, based on a narration from Abu Hanifa about fanning. A fifth saying: action intended by the doer as a separate act. The commentator notes that the second saying (observer's opinion) is not distinct from the first (hand movement) because customarily hand movement leads the observer to think the person is not in prayer. Similarly, the three-movement criterion is subsumed. The majority therefore chose the combination of the first and second sayings as the operative definitions.

Key Terms
العمل الكثير (al-amal al-kathir): Action that is considered much or excessive, which invalidates salah if it is not part of the prayer's own actions or its rectification. The text discusses multiple Hanafi definitions without settling on a single one.
الناظر (al-nazir): The observer referred to in the second definition: a person who does not know that the worshipper has commenced salah, and from a distance, such that if he sees the action he would conclude the doer is not in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (al-jumhur), which definition(s) of al-amal al-kathir are relied upon for fatwa?
A) Only the third saying: what the worshipper deems much.
B) Only the fourth saying: three consecutive movements.
C) The combination of the first and second sayings: what requires both hands and what an observer knows is not prayer.
D) The fifth saying: action intended as a separate act.

Key Takeaway
Excessive action in prayer is defined by multiple Hanafi criteria; the operative ruling combines hand-movement and observer-perception tests.

The Hanafi Criterion for Excessive Action in Prayer
Defining the legal criterion for excessive action that invalidates prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE and EXPLAIN the Hanafi criterion for excessive action that invalidates prayer, using the observer's perception as the primary measure.

Core Idea
Excessive action is what makes an observer think you are not praying—not a fixed number of movements.

Prayer demands stillness, but not every movement breaks it. The Hanafi school defines a threshold: actions that lead an uninformed observer to conclude you are not in prayer. This lesson unpacks that definition from the classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in question
The passage defines 'excessive action' (al-‘amal al-kathīr) in the context of salah. What type of action is being discussed? (Answer: actions that could potentially nullify the prayer if they are 'much'.)
The act is not specified; it is a category.
2
State the primary definition
The matn reports: 'what an observer knows that the doer is not in prayer'. This is the dominant Hanafi view. Note that 'knows' includes strong conjecture (ẓann), as clarified in commentaries.
al-Ghazzi in Tanwir al-Absar explains 'knows' as 'does not doubt'.
3
Analyze the observer condition
The observer is one who does not know the person has started prayer, and is at a distance. Why distance? Because a close observer can see the intention or posture. The criterion is what appears to a typical onlooker.
al-Nahr and al-Bada'i' restrict to 'from a distance'.
4
Contrast with alternative views
The passage also notes a second view: what the worshipper deems excessive. Al-Sarakhsi favored this as closer to Abu Hanifa's method, but the majority rejected it as subjective. Understand the basis for preferring the objective observer test.
The commentator of al-Munyah criticized the second view as unstandardized.

Chapter of Prayer: What invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. [1] And it was said: (The excessive action is) that which an observer knows that the doer is not a person in prayer, and the majority of teachers are upon this. And it was said: (It is) what the worshipper deems excessive. Imam al-Sarakhsi said: This is closer to the madhhab of Abu Hanifa, for his custom is to refer to the opinion of the one afflicted (by the action). (1) [1] His statement: 'And it was said'; this statement was declared correct in al-Bada'i', al-Tabyin, and al-Walwaljiyyah. And in al-Muhit it is said: It is the best. And al-Sadr al-Shahid said it is the correct view. And in al-Khaniyyah and al-Khulasah it is the choice of the general scholars. (2) His statement: 'that which an observer knows'; the intended meaning of 'knowledge' includes conjecture, as al-Ghazzi indicated in Tanwir al-Absar, where he said: That which the observer does not doubt about its doer that he is not in it (prayer). And the intended meaning of 'observer' is one who does not have knowledge of the person starting prayer, as in al-Bahr and al-Halabah. Hence, it was restricted in al-Nahr and al-Bada'i' by his statement 'from a distance', based on the fact that for a close observer the situation is not usually hidden. (3) His statement: 'This is closer...' means that the method of Abu Hanifa in such a situation where no measure has come from the Lawgiver is to refer to the opinion and conjecture of the one afflicted with that matter. So this interpretation agrees with his method in similar cases. (4) The commentator of al-Munyah said: But it is not standardized, and referring the like of this to the opinion of the common people is not proper. And most of the subsidiary cases or all of them are based on the first two: i.e., that which requires two hands, and that by which the observer knows he is not a person in prayer. The first (view). And it appears that the second statement, i.e., the one mentioned first in this commentary, is not outside the meaning mentioned here second, because that which is typically performed with two hands usually makes the observer strongly conjecture that he is not in prayer, and similarly the statement of one who considers repetition three times; therefore the majority chose it.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage defines the legal criterion for 'excessive action' (al- amal al-kathir) that invalidates prayer. The matn presents three opinions: (1) what an observer knows makes the doer appear not in prayer, (2) what the worshipper deems excessive, and (3) what requires two hands (the first) or repetition three times. The commentary notes that the first opinion is upheld by the majority of teachers (ʿāmmat al-mashāyikh), and al-Sarakhsi considers the second closer to Abu Hanifa's approach of delegating to the affected person's judgment. However, the commentator of al-Munyah objects that this is not standardized and should not be left to laypeople. The final observation reconciles the opinions, noting that actions performed with two hands (like large movements) usually signal to an observer that the person is not in prayer, hence the majority's preference. The passage thus establishes the Hanafi doctrine that excessive action is gauged by external appearance and custom, not by a fixed number.

Key Terms
العمل الكثير (al-‘amal al-kathīr): Action that invalidates prayer because it makes an observer believe the doer is not in prayer; defined by custom, not a fixed number.
الناظر (al-nāẓir): An observer who does not know that the person has entered prayer; from a distance, the observer's strong conjecture that the person is not praying is the criterion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Any movement performed three times in succession.
B) The action's apparent effect on an observer who does not know the person is in prayer.
C) The worshipper's personal feeling of having done too much.
D) The action's similarity to eating or drinking.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of excessive action rests on the observer's perception, not a fixed count, making it custom-dependent.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Observer vs. Worshipper's Judgment
Defining the legal criterion for excessive action that invalidates prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to IDENTIFY the two Hanafi positions on defining excessive action, COMPARE their proofs, and STATE the muftā bihī (operative ruling).

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa's preference for the worshipper's own judgment was set aside by later scholars for the objective observer test.

While the majority of Hanafi scholars define excessive action by what an observer sees, a minority view attributed to Abu Hanifa himself refers to the worshipper's personal assessment. This lesson delves into the debate, each side's reasoning, and why the majority view prevailed.

Guided Path
1
State the two views
View 1: Excessive action is what an observer (from a distance) perceives as indicating non-prayer. View 2: Excessive action is what the worshipper personally deems excessive. These are both reported in the matn.
2
Identify the authority for each
View 1 is attributed to the 'majority of teachers' (ʿāmmat al-mashāyikh). View 2 is attributed to Abu Hanifa's method, as al-Sarakhsi explains: 'this is closer to the madhhab of Abu Hanifa.'
Al-Sarakhsi is a leading authority; his preference carries weight.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind View 2
Abu Hanifa's principle is to defer to the one directly affected (al-mubtalā bihī) in matters where the Lawgiver set no specific standard. The worshipper's own dominant conjecture (ẓann) should determine whether the action is excessive.
This aligns with Abu Hanifa's practice in other undefined areas.
4
Examine the critique of View 2
The commentator of al-Munyah objects: it is not standardized (ghayr maḍbūṭ) and should not be left to laypeople. Hanafi law requires consistency and predictability in fatwa.
The critique is methodological: subjectivity undermines legal uniformity.
5
Conclude with the tarjih
Despite al-Sarakhsi's preference, the majority of later scholars (including al-Marghinani, al-Haskafi) chose the observer test. The reasoning: actions that typically require two hands (like sweeping, large gestures) are precisely those that cause an observer to doubt, thus merging the two views in practice. Therefore, the muftā bihī is the observer test.
The final paragraph of the passage reconciles the views, noting that actions performed with two hands are what the observer would notice.

Ruling on Starting Another Prayer While in the Midst of One
Commentary on matn about renewing intention in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a person begins a second prayer while already in the first, and identify the two scenarios with their consequences.

Core Idea
Starting a second prayer invalidates the first; the second must be completed from scratch.

This passage from `Umdat al-Ri`ayah explains a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: you cannot be in two prayers at once. The commentator unpacks a concise matn and addresses the precise conditions and a disputed point about raising hands. We will walk through the scenarios so you can apply them confidently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible scenarios
Determine whether the person renews intention for a different prayer or for the same prayer he is already in.
The ruling differs drastically; the first scenario invalidates the first prayer.
2
Apply the ruling for a different prayer
If the new intention is for a different prayer (even if similar), the first prayer becomes invalid (batil) and the second must be completed from the beginning. The rak`ah already prayed does not count.
This applies even if less than a full rak`ah was prayed.
3
Apply the ruling for the same prayer
If the new intention is for the same prayer, the first prayer remains valid. The re-intention is legally ineffective; he continues from where he left off.
The rak`ah already prayed counts toward completion.
4
Recognize the effect of verbal intention
If the person expresses the intention aloud, that utterance is considered speech (kalam) and invalidates the entire prayer, regardless of which prayer is intended.
Silent intention is the norm; speaking it is a separate invalidating act.
5
Address the controversial point about raising hands
Some earlier Hanafis claimed that raising hands during the renewal of intention invalidates, but the correct and relied-upon position is that it does not.
The commentator explicitly dismisses the opposing view as weak and rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من صلي ركعة ثم شرع ، صلّى كَمُلاً إن شرع في أُخرى وإلا أتم الأولى : أي إن صلى ركعة من صلاة، ثُمَّ شَرَع: أي نَوَى وجدد التحريمة من غير رفع اليدين، فإن شرع في صلاةٍ أُخرى يُتِمَّ هذه الأخرى، ولا يحتسب منها الركعة التي صلاها، وإن شرع في الصَّلاة الأولى، فالركعة التي صلاها محسوبة، فيتم الأولى. [١) قوله : ركعة ... الخ ؛ هذا قيد اتفاقي، فإنّ الحكم لا يختلف فيما دونها أيضاً. (٢) قوله : في أخرى؛ أي مغايرة للأولى، ولو من وجه، فلو صلى ركعة من الظهر مثلاً ثم افتتح العصر أو التطوع بتكبيرة، وجب عليه أن يتم الثانية، وفسدت الأولى بشروعه في الأخرى ، ولو كان منفرداً فكبر ينوي الاقتداء أو عكسه فسد الأوّل، وكذا لو نوى واجباً أو كان شرع في صلاة جنازة فجيء بأخرى ، فكبّر ينويهما أو الثانية. كذا في «الفتح القدير». (۳) قوله : وإلا أتم الأولى؛ أي إن لم يشرع في الأخرى، بل جدّد التحريمة للأولى بعدما صلّى قدراً منها لا تفسد الأولى، فيتمها، ويجزئ بما صلّى سابقاً، فإنه نوى الشروع في عين ما هو فيه، فلغت نيته، وبقي المنوي على حاله، فلو صلّى من الظهرِ ركعة ، ثمّ نوى ثانياً لتلك الصلاةِ يُصلّي بعدها ثلاث ركعات. ولو صلّى أربعاً على ظنّ أنّ الأولى فسدت ، ولم يقعد في الثالثة فسدت صلاته ؛ لأنه ترك القعدة الأخيرة، وهذا كله إذا نوى بقلبه. أمـا لـو نـوى بلسانه بأن قال : نويت أن أصلّي الظهر انتقض ما صَلَّى، ولا يجزئ بتلك الركعة ؛ لأنه كلام مفسد. كذا في «الخلاصة». [٤] قوله : من غير رفع اليدين ؛ هذا قيد اتفاقي، ذكره لتكون المسألة اتفاقية لا خلاف فيها بين أصحابنا أصلا، فإن منهم مَن صَرَّحَ بأنّ رفع اليدين في أثناء الصلاة مفسد، وقد عرفت أنه قول شاد مردود ، فلو جدّد التحريمة مع رفع اليدين أيضاً، فالحكم هو ما ذكره، فإنّ رفع اليدين غير مفسد على القول الصحيح الذي ليس ما سواء إلا غلطاً. [0] قوله : ولا يحتسب ؛ لأنه لَمَّا شَرَعَ في الأخرى بطلت الأولى، فإنّه لا يمكن أداء صلاتين مغايرتين معا فبالضرورة تبطل الأولى؛ لوجود ما ينافيها بعدها.
Whoever prays one rak'ah and then starts (another prayer), he prays completely if he starts another; otherwise he completes the first. Meaning: If he prays one rak'ah of a prayer, then starts – i.e., intends and renews the tahrimah without raising the hands – then if he starts another prayer, he must complete this other one, and the rak'ah he had prayed is not counted from it. If he starts in the first prayer, the rak'ah he prayed is counted, so he completes the first. [1] (His statement: 'rak'ah' etc. – this is a conventional condition; the ruling does not differ for less than that.) [2] (His statement: 'in another' – meaning different from the first, even in one aspect. For example, if he prayed a rak'ah of Zuhr, then started Asr or a supererogatory prayer with a takbir, he must complete the second, and the first is invalidated by his starting the other. If he was alone and said takbir intending to follow (an imam) or vice versa, the first is invalidated. Likewise, if he intended a mandatory prayer or had started a funeral prayer and another was brought, and he said takbir intending both or the second. This is stated in al-Fath al-Qadir.) [3] (His statement: 'otherwise he completes the first' – meaning if he did not start another, but rather renewed the tahrimah for the first after having prayed some of it, the first is not invalidated; he completes it and it suffices with what he prayed earlier. For he intended to start the very same one he was in, so his intention becomes void, and the intended (prayer) remains as it was. So if he prayed a rak'ah of Zuhr, then intended a second time for that same prayer, he prays three rak'ahs after that. If he prayed four assuming the first was invalid and did not sit for the third, his prayer becomes invalid because he abandoned the final sitting. All this if he intends with his heart. If he intends with his tongue by saying: 'I intend to pray Zuhr', what he prayed is broken and that rak'ah does not suffice because it is speech that invalidates. This is stated in al-Khulasa.) [4] (His statement: 'without raising the hands' – this is a conventional condition, mentioned to make the issue agreed upon with no dispute among our companions at all. For some of them explicitly said that raising the hands during prayer invalidates, and you have come to know that it is a weak, rejected statement. So if he renews the tahrimah with raising hands as well, the ruling is what he mentioned, for raising the hands does not invalidate according to the correct view, which has no alternative but error.) [0] (His statement: 'and it is not counted' – because when he started the other, the first became invalid; for it is impossible to perform two different prayers simultaneously, so necessarily the first is invalidated due to the existence of what contradicts it after it.)
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn 'Whoever prays one rak'ah and then starts, he prays completely if he starts another; otherwise he completes the first.' The commentator explains the ruling: if a person has prayed a part (e.g., one rak'ah) of an obligatory prayer and then intends to begin a different prayer (by renewing the tahrimah), two scenarios arise. First, if he starts a different prayer (e.g., switches from Zuhr to Asr, or to a supererogatory prayer), he is obliged to complete the second prayer; the first prayer becomes invalid because one cannot validly engage in two distinct prayers simultaneously. The rak'ah already performed does not count toward the second prayer. Second, if he does not start a different prayer but rather renews the intention for the very same prayer (e.g., doubts his first intention and re-intends), the first prayer remains valid, and he continues from where he left off; the re-intention is legally ineffective (لغو) and the original prayer continues. The passage then addresses several detailed issues. The word 'rak'ah' is a conventional condition; the same ruling applies even if less than a full rak'ah was prayed. The phrase 'in another' means a prayer that is different in kind, even if similar in some aspects; examples include switching from fard to sunnah or from one fard to another, and even switching from being an imam to being a follower or vice versa. The rule also applies when two funeral prayers are brought. The commentator then discusses intention: if the renewal of intention is done silently in the heart, the rulings above apply; if it is expressed verbally (uttering the intention aloud), that utterance is considered speech that invalidates the prayer, and the entire prayer becomes invalid. A notable intra-Hanafi issue is raised about raising the hands during the new tahrimah. Some earlier Hanafi authorities considered raising hands during prayer to be invalidating, but the commentator (following the majority and the relied-upon view) states that this opinion is weak and rejected; hence the ruling remains the same whether or not the hands are raised. The rational basis is that it is impossible to properly perform two different prayers at once, so starting the second necessarily voids the first.

Key Terms
تحريمة (tahrimah): The opening takbir and intention that initiates a prayer.
ركعة (rak'ah): A unit of prayer consisting of standing, bowing, and prostrations.
فساد (fasad): Invalidation of the prayer requiring restart.
قول شاد مردود (qawl shadhdh mardud): An opinion that is irregular and rejected within the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person prays one rak'ah of Zuhr, then intends and says 'I now begin Asr' without raising his hands, what is the ruling?
A) Both prayers are valid; he completes Zuhr first then Asr.
B) The Zuhr becomes invalid; he must complete the Asr from the beginning.
C) The Asr is invalid; he continues Zuhr.
D) He may choose which prayer to complete.

Key Takeaway
Starting a different prayer while in the midst of another invalidates the first; the second must be completed from the beginning, and the rak`ah already prayed does not count.

Actions That Do Not Invalidate the Prayer: A Commentary Analysis
Commentary listing and explaining actions that do not invalidate prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the Hanafi rulings for five actions that do not invalidate prayer (crying from piety, clearing throat with excuse, supplication to Allah, little action, passing of anyone) and cite the primary hadith evidence for the passing ruling.

Core Idea
A worshipper's prayer remains valid despite crying from piety, clearing throat, supplicating to Allah, small movements, or someone passing in front.

In this lesson, we explore a key passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah commenting on actions that do not invalidate the prayer. These rulings are grounded in the principle that only excessive, unrelated movements or worldly speech break the prayer. By studying each action, you will learn how Hanafi fiqh balances devotion with human imperfection.

Guided Path
1
List the five non-invalidating actions
Read the first line of the passage: 'And his crying from remembrance of Paradise or Hell does not invalidate it, nor clearing the throat with an excuse, nor supplication that does not ask of people, nor little action, nor the passing of anyone.' Commit these five to memory.
This is the matn being commented on.
2
Analyze the criterion for crying
The commentator distinguishes crying from khushu' (valid) vs crying from worldly pain (invalid). Understand: the intention matters. Crying from piety implies supplication, which is allowed. Crying from complaint resembles human speech.
Hanafis argue this is qiyas: permissible dua vs prohibited human talk.
3
Examine clearing throat and dua
Clearing the throat is excused if needed; dua must be directed only to Allah. These are allowed because they do not constitute 'speech of people' (kalam al-nas).
Dua to Allah is worship; clearing throat for recitation is a necessity.
4
Define little action
Little action is known by its opposite, much action, which varies by interpretation. Key: small movements performed by the Prophet and Companions are permissible and do not invalidate.
See the reference to the six authentic books for evidence.
5
Confirm the ruling on passing
The hadith 'لا يقطع الصلاة شيء' (Nothing cuts off the prayer) is the primary proof. The commentator adds that the worshipper should still try to prevent passersby, but the prayer remains valid.
This is a zanni dalil, but Hanafis prioritize it over other rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يفسدها بكاؤه من ذكر الجنة ، أو النار، والتنحنح بعذر، والدعاء بما لا يسأل من الناس، والعمل القليل، وهو ضدُّ الكثير على اختلاف الأقوال، ومرور أحد [١] قوله : من ذكر الجنة والنار ؛ أي وما يماثلهما، فإن البكاء إذا كان من أمر الآخرة دل على كمال الخشوع، وفيه تعريض بسؤال الجنَّة والتعوذ من النار، ولو صرّح به بأن قال : اللهم إني اسألك الجنّة وأعوذ بك من النار لا تفسد، فكيف بما يشير إليه بخلاف البكاء من وجع أو مصيبة، فإنّ فيه إظهار الجزع والتأسف ، فكان من كلام الناس. كذا قال العيني [٢]قوله : والعمل القليل؛ فقد ورد عن النبي الله وأصحابه أنهم ارتكبوا أعمالاً في الصلاة ولم يضرّ ذلك بصلاتهم كما لا يخفى على ناظر الصحاح الستة وغيرها. [٣] قوله : وهو ضد الكثير على اختلاف الأقوال؛ يعني القليل يعرفُ بالكثير على الأقوال الواردة في تفسيره. [٤]قوله : ومرور أحد، عطف على قوله : «بكاؤه»، كقرينة السابق؛ أي لا يفسدها مرور أحد أمام المصلّي رجلاً كان أو امرأة، إنساناً كان أو حيواناً، فلو قال : مرور شيء كان أولى، وذلك لحديث: «لا يقطع الصلاة شيء، وادرؤا ما استطعتم»، أخرجه أبو داود والدارقطني والطبراني وغيرهم، وله شواهد مروية في الصحيحين وغيرهما. وعن أبي أمامة الله قال : لا يقطع الصلاة شيء» قال الهيثمي في «مجمع الزوائد» : رواه الطبراني في الكبير وإسناده حسن». وعن أبي ذر قال : «لا يقطع الصلاة شيء إذا كان بين يديك كآخرة الرحل أو كواسطة الرحل» في «مسند أبي عوانة».
And his crying from remembrance of Paradise or Hell does not invalidate it, nor clearing the throat with an excuse, nor supplication that does not ask of people, nor little action – and it is the opposite of much action according to different sayings – nor the passing of anyone. [1] His statement: 'from remembrance of Paradise and Hell' – i.e., and what resembles them. For crying, if it is due to matters of the Hereafter, indicates perfection of humility, and it implicitly involves asking for Paradise and seeking refuge from Hell. If one explicitly says: 'O Allah, I ask You for Paradise and seek refuge with You from Hell,' it does not invalidate, so how about what alludes to it? Unlike crying from pain or calamity, for that shows agitation and regret, so it is from the speech of people. Thus said al-Ayni. [2] His statement: 'and little action' – it has been related from the Prophet ﷺ and his Companions that they performed actions in prayer and that did not harm their prayers, as is not hidden from the observer of the six authentic books and others. [3] His statement: 'And it is the opposite of much action according to different sayings' – meaning little action is known by its opposite, according to the sayings transmitted in its interpretation. [4] His statement: 'and the passing of anyone' – it is conjoined to his statement 'his crying' as a preceding context; i.e., the passing of anyone in front of the worshipper does not invalidate, whether man or woman, human or animal. It would have been better if he said 'passing of something.' This is because of the hadith: 'Nothing cuts off the prayer, and repel (it) as much as you can' – narrated by Abu Dawood, al-Daraqutni, al-Tabarani and others. It has supporting narrations in the Sahihs and others. And from Abu Umamah (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: 'Nothing cuts off the prayer.' Al-Haythami in Majma' al-Zawa'id said: 'Narrated by al-Tabarani in al-Kabir with a good chain.' And from Abu Dharr: 'Nothing cuts off the prayer if there is the back of a saddle or the middle of a saddle in front of you.' In Musnad Abi 'Awanah.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the matn that lists actions which do not invalidate the prayer. The commentator elaborates each item. First, crying: if it arises from contemplation of Paradise, Hell, or other matters of the Hereafter, it reflects khushu' (humility) and does not invalidate; even explicit supplication for Paradise or refuge from Hell is permissible. In contrast, crying due to worldly pain or calamity shows impatience and resembles ordinary speech, thus invalidates. Second, clearing the throat (tanahnuh) is excused and not invalidating if done for a valid reason (e.g., to recorrect recitation). Third, dua that does not involve asking people (i.e., requests that only Allah can grant) is allowed; it is a form of worship. Fourth, 'little action' (amal qalil) – the commentator notes that it is defined by contrast with 'much action', on which there are differences of opinion among scholars. The Prophet and the Companions performed small movements in prayer, and those did not invalidate. Fifth, someone passing in front of the worshipper (murur ahad) does not invalidate the prayer, regardless of who or what passes (man, woman, animal). The commentator points out that the matn could have said 'passing of something' to be more inclusive. The evidence is the hadith 'Nothing cuts off the prayer' (la yaqta'u al-salat shay') with the addition 'and repel it as much as you can' (wadra'u ma istata'tum). This hadith is reported by Abu Dawood and others, with supporting narrations from Abu Umamah and Abu Dharr. Thus the Hanafi position is that the prayer is not invalidated by any of these; instead, one should gently prevent the passersby if possible, but the prayer remains valid even if they pass.

Key Terms
بكاء (buka'): Crying in prayer; if from fear of the afterlife, it does not invalidate; if from worldly pain, it does.
تنحنح (tanahnuh): Clearing the throat; if done with an excuse (e.g., need to correct recitation), it does not invalidate.
عمل قليل (amal qalil): A small action that does not break the continuity of prayer; defined by the consensus of scholars based on practice of the Prophet and Companions.
مرور أحد (murur ahad): The passing of someone or something in front of the worshipper; does not invalidate the prayer, though the worshipper should try to prevent it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a person who cries in prayer from fear of Hell?
A) The prayer is invalid because crying breaks the concentration.
B) The prayer is valid because crying from piety is like supplication and does not invalidate.
C) The prayer becomes makruh but is valid.
D) The prayer is invalid unless the crying is quiet.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms that prayer is not invalidated by crying from piety, clearing the throat with excuse, supplicating to Allah, small movements, or anyone passing in front—each with specific conditions.

What Does Not Invalidate Prayer in the Hanafi School
Ruling with proof and linguistic note

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five actions that do not invalidate prayer according to the Hanafi school and explain the proof for each.

Core Idea
Prayer remains valid despite crying from fear of Allah, clearing throat with excuse, permissible supplication, little action, or someone passing in front.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on the matn of al-Wiqayah, listing five situations that do not break the prayer. The commentator clarifies that crying from worldly matters would invalidate, while crying from spiritual emotion does not. The hadith 'Nothing cuts off the prayer' provides the basis for the ruling on passing. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for maintaining valid prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five actions
List the five actions mentioned in the matn: crying from remembrance of Paradise or Hellfire, clearing the throat with an excuse, supplication for what is permissible, little action, and the passing of anyone in front of the worshiper.
These are exceptions to the general rule that extraneous actions invalidate prayer.
2
Understand the reasoning for crying
Crying from spiritual matters indicates humility and is an indirect supplication, so it does not invalidate. Crying from worldly pain shows distress and is like human speech, thus invalidates.
The commentary contrasts 'from the afterlife' with 'from pain or calamity'.
3
Define 'little action'
Little action is defined by contrast with much action, which is judged by custom or scholarly consensus. The Prophet and companions performed small actions in prayer without harm.
The threshold varies; the text does not give a fixed measure.
4
Analyze the proof for passing
The hadith 'لا يقطع الصلاة شيء' (Nothing cuts off prayer) is cited. The Hanafi school interprets this literally: passing does not invalidate. However, the passer sins if there is no barrier, based on another hadith warning of the sin.
This ruling is specific to Hanafi; other schools may consider certain passers as invalidators.

[٣]قوله : بالكسر ؛ أي بكسر العين، قال المجد في «القاموس»: «المسجد كمسكن الجبهة، والآراب السبعة مساجد، والمسجد أي بكسر الجيم: مكان، وتفتح جيمه، والمَفْعَل من باب نَصَر بفتح العين : اسماً كان أو مصدراً إلا أحرفاً : كَمَسجِد ومطلع ومَشْرِق ومَسقِط ومَفْرِق ومَجْزر ومسكن ومرفق ومنبت ومنسك ألزموها كسر العين، والفتح جائز، وإن لم نسمعه». انتهى (٤) قوله : أرادوا ؛ لأن فيه بقاء للمشهور على ما اصطلح عليه الجمهور، ولما لم یكـن بـد مـن الفرق بينه وبين المعنى الآخر دفعاً للالتباس اختاروا استعمال ما هو على القياس في المعنى الآخر.
(۲)
[0]قوله : على القياس ؛ أي على ما هو مقتضى القياس، وهو فتح العين من المفعل من باب نصر ينصر.
(۱) «الموطأ» (۱ : ١٥٤) ، و (سنن أبي داود (١): ٢٤٤) ، و(صحیح ابن خزيمة (٢ : ١٤)،
وغيرها.
(۲) من القاموس المحيط» (۱ : ۲۸۳).
Book of Prayer: What invalidates prayer and what is disliked therein. And it is not invalidated by his crying from remembrance of Paradise or Hellfire, and clearing the throat with an excuse, and supplication with what is not asked of people, and little action, and the passing of someone. (Commentary: His crying from remembrance of Paradise and Hellfire means and what resembles them, for crying if it is from matters of the Hereafter indicates complete humility, and it contains an indirect request for Paradise and seeking refuge from Hellfire, and if he explicitly says: 'O Allah, I ask You for Paradise and seek refuge with You from Hellfire,' it does not invalidate, so how about what is implied? Unlike crying from pain or calamity, for that shows distress and regret, so it is from the speech of people. And little action: it has been reported from the Prophet and his companions that they performed actions in prayer and that did not harm their prayers. And passing of someone: meaning it does not invalidate the passing of anyone in front of the worshiper, be it a man or woman, human or animal. And the forsaker sins if he passes in his place of prostration on the ground without a barrier. The word 'masjid' is from the pattern maf'il with kasr, and it is permissible to open the letter on analogy. The jurists: if they say with fatha they mean the place of prostration, and if they say with kasra they mean the well-known meaning (mosque). They continued on the analogy for the first meaning.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of 'Al-Wiqayah' regarding what invalidates prayer. The commentator expands on the ruling that crying from the remembrance of Paradise or Hellfire does not break the prayer, as it indicates humility and is an indirect supplication. The proof is that even explicit supplication for Paradise does not invalidate. In contrast, crying due to worldly pain or calamity is considered human speech and would invalidate. Next, 'little action' is defined negatively by reference to 'much action' about which there are different opinions; the Prophet and his companions performed small actions in prayer without harm. Then, the passage states that someone passing in front of the worshiper does not invalidate the prayer, citing the hadith 'Nothing cuts off the prayer; repel as much as you can.' The commentary also discusses that the passer sins if he passes in the place of prostration without a barrier, based on the severe warning hadith. Finally, a linguistic note: the word 'masjid' originally means place of prostration (with fatha on the 'ayn), but the common usage (mosque) is with kasra; the jurists adopted the fatha for the place of prostration and kasra for the mosque to avoid confusion.

Key Terms
العمل القليل (al-'amal al-qalil): An action in prayer that is considered little, as opposed to much; its definition varies among scholars but is typically judged by custom.
مرور أحد (murur ahad): The passing of any person or animal in front of the worshiper during prayer.
مسجد (masjid (with fatḥa) / masjid (with kasra)): With fatḥa: the physical place of prostration (on the ground). With kasra: the well-known mosque building.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following does NOT invalidate the prayer?
A) Crying from a worldly calamity
B) Clearing the throat with an excuse
C) Passing a woman in front of the worshiper
D) Speaking intentionally without excuse

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that five specific actions do not invalidate prayer: spiritual crying, throat-clearing with excuse, permissible supplication, little action, and passing in front; the passer, however, sins if without a barrier.

Passing in Front of a Person Praying: Ruling and Scope
Commentary expanding a ruling with linguistic and practical detail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on passing before a praying person, distinguish the varying scope based on mosque size, and apply the ruling to specific scenarios.

Core Idea
Passing before a praying person is sinful, but its scope depends on whether the mosque is small or large.

Passing in front of someone who is praying is a serious act, but its sinfulness is not absolute. The Hanafi school, drawing on a powerful hadith and careful linguistic analysis, distinguishes between small mosques where any passing is sinful and large mosques where only passing the place of prostration incurs sin. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its evidence, and the reasoning behind this nuance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Base Ruling
Start with the core statement: passing before a praying person without a barrier is sinful (haram). Note the hadith proof that warns of severe sin.
'Haram' here means it is a grave sin, not merely disliked.
2
Understand the Linguistic Distinction
The term 'masjid' can mean either the whole mosque (kasra on 'ayn) or the specific place of prostration (fatha). The jurists use the fatha form to refer to the place of prostration, which becomes key in defining the prohibited area.
This linguistic nuance is unique to Hanafi juristic usage and avoids ambiguity.
3
Define the Barrier
The ruling applies only when there is no barrier (ha'il) between the passer and the worshiper. A barrier like a pillar or wall removes the sin. Identify what qualifies as a barrier.
The text mentions 'pillar, wall, and the like'.
4
Analyze the Scope Based on Mosque Size
The critical detail: in a small mosque, the entire area before the worshiper is considered the place of prostration, so any passing is sinful. In a large mosque or open desert, only passing directly in the place of prostration (from the worshiper's feet to the qibla wall) incurs sin.
The small mosque is defined as less than 40-60 cubits; the preferred view is 40 cubits.
5
Identify the Preferred View and Authorities
The preferred view (mufta bihi) is the restricted scope in large mosques, chosen by al-Nasafi, al-Hidayah, al-Sarakhsi, and others. Note the reasoning: to avoid hardship for passersby.
Some early mashayikh held an absolute prohibition, but the majority prefer the nuanced view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويأثم إن مر في مسجده على الأرض بلا حائل. المَسْجِدُ من الألفاظ التي جاءت على المفعل بالكسر، ويجوز فيها الفتح على القياس، والفقهاء إذا قالوا: بالفتح أرادوا موضع السجود، وإن قالوا بالكسر أرادوا: المعنى المشهور، فإنَّهم لم يجدوا الكسر، وهو خلافُ القياس إلا في المعنى المشهور، ففي المعنى الأوَّل استمروا على القياس.
قوله: ويأثم؛ أي المارّ بين يدي المصلّي كما يدلّ عليه قوله: «لو علم المار بين يدي المصلّي ماذا عليه من الوزر لكان أن يقف أربعين - أي سنة خيراً من أن يمر بين يديه»، أخرجه مالك وأصحاب الصحاح وغيرهم، وفي الباب أخبار كثيرة مبسوطة في مواضعها.
قوله: حائل المسجد؛ أي شيء يحول بين المار وبين المصلي كالإسطوانة والجدار ونحو ذلك.
قوله: بالكسر؛ أي بكسر العين، قال المجد في «القاموس»: «المسجد كمسكن الجبهة، والآراب السبعة مساجد، والمسجد أي بكسر الجيم: مكان، وتفتح جيمه، والمَفْعَل من باب نَصَر بفتح العين: اسماً كان أو مصدراً إلا أحرفاً: كَمَسجِد ومطلع ومَشْرِق ومَسقِط ومَفْرِق ومَجْزر ومسكن ومرفق ومنبت ومنسك ألزموها كسر العين، والفتح جائز، وإن لم نسمعه». انتهى.
قوله: أرادوا؛ لأن فيه بقاء للمشهور على ما اصطلح عليه الجمهور، ولما لم يكن بد من الفرق بينه وبين المعنى الآخر دفعاً للالتباس اختاروا استعمال ما هو على القياس في المعنى الآخر.
قوله: على القياس؛ أي على ما هو مقتضى القياس، وهو فتح العين من المفعل من باب نصر ينصر.
وفي تفسير موضع السجود تفصيل، فاعلم أنَّ الصَّلاةَ إن كانت في المسجد الصغير، فالمرور أمام المصلي حيث كان يوجب الإثم؛ لأنَّ المسجد الصغير مكان واحد، فأَمَامَ المصلّي حيث كان في حكم موضع السجود، وإن كانت في المسجد الكبير، أو في الصحراء: فعند بعض المشايخ: إن مرَّ في موضع السجود يأثم.
قوله: موضع السجود؛ فإنّه لو كان المراد المعنى المشهور، لزم بإطلاقه لزوم الإثم لمن مرَّ أمام المصلي في المسجد مطلقاً، ولم يقل به أحد، وأيضاً على تقدير إرادته لا يعلم حكم الصحراء من المتن.
قوله: الصغير؛ هو أقل من ستين ذراعاً، وقيل: من أربعين، وهو المختار كما في «جامع الرموز» عن «الجواهر».
قوله: أمام المصلي؛ أي من موضع قدميه إلى حائط القبلة، وكذلك الحكم في الدار والبيت.
قوله: مكان واحد؛ قال في «رد المحتار»: «أي من حيث إنّه لم يجعل الفاصل فيه بقدر صفين مانعاً من الاقتداء، تنزيلاً له منزلة مكان واحد، بخلاف المسجد الكبير، فإنّه جُعِلَ فيه مانعاً، فكذا هاهنا يجعلُ ما بين يدي المصلي إلى حائط القبلة مكاناً واحداً بخلاف المسجد الكبير والصحراء، فإنّه لو جعل كذلك لزم الحرج على المارة، فاقتصر على موضع السجود».
قوله: فعند بعض المشايخ؛ هذا هو الذي اختاره النَّسفي في «الكنز» وصححه في «الكافي»، وهو مختار صاحب «الهداية»، وشمس الأئمة السرخسي، وقاضي خان، وفي «المحيط»: هو أحسن؛ لأنَّ ذلك القدر أي من موضع قدمه إلى حائط القبلة هو موضع السجود.
And he sins if he passes in his mosque on the ground without a barrier. 'Al-masjid' is from words that come on the pattern 'maf'il' with kasra, and it is permissible to open it according to analogy. The jurists, when they say: with fatha, they intend the place of prostration, and if they say with kasra, they intend the well-known meaning. For they did not find the kasra, and it is contrary to analogy except in the well-known meaning. So in the first meaning they continued upon analogy. His saying: 'and he sins' – i.e., the one passing in front of the praying person, as indicated by his saying: 'If the one passing in front of the praying person knew what burden (of sin) is upon him, he would rather stand for forty – i.e., years – than pass in front of him.' Reported by Malik and the authors of the Sahih collections and others. And there are many reports on this subject, detailed in their places. His saying: 'a barrier of the mosque' – i.e., anything that intervenes between the passer and the worshiper, like a pillar, wall, and the like. His saying: 'with kasra' – i.e., with kasra on the 'ayn. Al-Majd said in 'al-Qamus': 'Al-masjid (with kasra on jim) is like maskan meaning the forehead, and the seven limbs of prostration are called masajid. And al-masjid (with kasra on the jim) means a place. Its jim is also opened. And maf'al from the verb nasara is with fatha on the 'ayn, whether noun or verbal noun, except for a few words: masjid, matla, mashriq, masqit, mafriq, majzar, maskan, mirfaq, mambit, mansik – they are obliged with kasra on the 'ayn, though fatha is permissible even if we have not heard it.' His saying: 'they intended' – because it preserves the well-known usage according to the convention of the majority. And since there was no avoiding a distinction between it and the other meaning to prevent confusion, they chose to use what is in accordance with analogy for the other meaning. His saying: 'upon analogy' – i.e., upon what analogy requires, which is the fatha of the 'ayn in maf'al from the paradigm nasara yansuru. And in the interpretation of 'place of prostration' there is detail. Know that if the prayer is in a small mosque, then passing in front of the worshiper anywhere necessitates sin; because the small mosque is one place, so the area in front of the worshiper wherever he is has the ruling of the place of prostration. And if it is in a large mosque, or in the open desert: According to some mashayikh, if he passes in the place of prostration he sins. His saying: 'place of prostration' – for if the well-known meaning were intended, it would necessitate absolutely that anyone who passes in front of the worshiper in the mosque would sin, and no one has held that. Also, on the assumption of intending that, the ruling for the desert would not be known from the text. His saying: 'the small' – it is less than sixty cubits, and it is said: forty, and this is the chosen view as in 'Jami' al-Rumuz' from 'al-Jawahir'. His saying: 'in front of the worshiper' – i.e., from his feet to the wall of the qibla, and likewise the ruling in a house and a dwelling. His saying: 'is one place' – said in 'Radd al-Muhtar': 'i.e., in the sense that no barrier the size of two rows has been made to prevent following (in prayer), treating it as one place. Unlike the large mosque, where it is made a barrier. So here too, the area between the feet of the worshiper and the qibla wall is considered one place, unlike the large mosque and the desert, for if it were so considered it would cause hardship for passersby, so it is restricted to the place of prostration.' His saying: 'according to some mashayikh' – this is what al-Nasafi chose in 'al-Kanz' and corrected in 'al-Kafi', and it is the choice of the author of 'al-Hidayah', Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, Qadi Khan, and in 'al-Muhit' it is 'ahsan'; because that amount – i.e., from his feet to the qibla wall – is the place of prostration.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on Sharh al-Wiqaya, is an expansion on the ruling regarding passing in front of a praying person. The type is a ruling with proof (hukm ma'a dalil). The author begins by stating the base ruling: a person commits a sin if he passes in front of a praying person in the mosque without any barrier. The commentary then clarifies the linguistic usage of 'masjid': when used with a fatha on the 'ayn, it refers specifically to the place of prostration (the spot where the forehead touches), whereas with a kasra it refers to the mosque as a whole. The jurists use the fatha form for the technical term 'place of prostration' to distinguish it from the common meaning of mosque. The hadith proof provided is a well-known narration: 'If the one passing before a praying person knew the burden of sin upon him, he would rather stand for forty years than pass before him.' This is reported by Malik and in the Sahih collections. The commentary then defines 'barrier' (ha'il) as anything that separates the passer from the worshiper, such as a pillar or wall. The key legal discussion follows: the ruling differs based on the size of the mosque. In a small mosque (less than forty or sixty cubits), the entire area in front of the worshiper is considered part of the place of prostration, so passing anywhere before him is sinful. In a large mosque or open desert, only passing directly in the place of prostration (from the worshiper's feet to the qibla wall) incurs sin. This is the preferred position of many Hanafi authorities, including al-Nasafi in al-Kanz, the author of al-Hidayah, al-Sarakhsi, Qadi Khan, and it is considered 'ahsan' (best) in al-Muhit. The rationale given in Radd al-Muhtar is that the small mosque is treated as a single space, similar to the rule for following the imam when the barrier is less than two rows. In large mosques, restricting the prohibition to the place of prostration avoids hardship for the many passersby. The passage thus provides a detailed, practical ruling with its linguistic and evidential basis.

Key Terms
مسجد (masjid): The passage distinguishes two meanings: with fatha on 'ayn (masjad) = place of prostration; with kasra (masjid) = the mosque building.
موضع السجود (mawdi' al-sujud): The area directly before the worshiper, from his feet to the qibla wall.
حائل (ha'il): An object that blocks the path or line of sight, such as a pillar, wall, or person, which removes the sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi position, when does a person commit a sin by passing in front of a worshiper in a large mosque?
A) Whenever they pass in front of the worshiper anywhere in the mosque.
B) Only if they pass in the place of prostration (from the worshiper's feet to the qibla wall).
C) Only if they pass between the worshiper and the imam.
D) Only if there is no barrier, regardless of location.

Key Takeaway
Passing before a praying person is sinful, but the sin is limited to the place of prostration in large mosques and open spaces, while in small mosques it applies everywhere before the worshiper.

Defining the Place of Prostration and the Ruling on Passing Before a Worshipper
Defining 'place of prostration' with intra-Hanafi disagreement on its scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on the definition of the prohibited area for passing in front of a worshipper and apply the mufta bihi view to different mosque sizes and raised platforms.

Core Idea
The prohibition of passing before a worshipper depends on the definition of 'place of prostration', which varies by mosque size.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya expands on the ruling about passing in front of a praying person. The key is defining 'place of prostration' (mawdi' al-sujud). Within the Hanafi school, later scholars differed: some limit the prohibited area to the actual spot from the worshipper's feet to the qibla wall, while others include the line of sight. Understanding this ikhtilaf and its basis in avoiding hardship is essential for correct practice and fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is passing in front of a worshipper during prayer. The ruling is that it is sinful (makruh tahrimi) within a defined area, but the prayer remains valid.
2
Define 'place of prostration' (mawdi' al-sujud)
This is the key term. In the matn, it is defined from the worshipper's feet to the qibla wall. However, the commentary explains that this definition is not absolute; it depends on the context.
The well-known meaning would imply sin for any passing in a mosque, which no scholar holds.
3
Distinguish small mosque vs. large mosque/desert
In a small mosque (less than 60 or 40 cubits), the entire area in front of the worshipper is considered one place, so any passing is sinful. In a large mosque or desert, the prohibition is limited to avoid hardship for passers-by.
The small mosque is treated as a single unit because a two-row gap does not prevent joining prayer (iqtida).
4
Compare the two Hanafi views
First view (mufta bihi): sin only if passing within the actual place of prostration (feet to qibla wall). Second view: sin also if passing within the worshipper's line of sight when looking at the prostration spot.
The first view is preferred by al-Nasafi, al-Hidayah, al-Sarakhsi, and Qadikhan.
5
Apply to the raised platform (dukkan) scenario
If the worshipper is on an elevated platform and someone passes underneath, according to the first view, the passer is not in the place of prostration, so no sin. According to the second view, if the passer is within the line of sight and his limbs align with the worshipper's, he sins.
This shows the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) between the two views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها [١] وفي تفسير موضع السجود تفصيل، فاعلم أنَّ الصَّلاةَ إن كانت في المسجدِ الصغير، فالمرور أمام المصلي حيث كان يوجب الإثم؛ لأنَّ المسجد الصغير مكان واحد، فأَمَامَ المصلّي حيث كان في حكم موضع السجود، وإن كانت في المسجد الكبير، أو في الصحراء: فعند بعض المشايخ: إن مرَّ في موضع السجود يأثم وإلا فلا. وعند البعض: الموضع الذي يقع عليه النظر إذا كان المصلي ناظراً في موضع سجوده له حكم موضع السجود، فيأثم بالمرور في ذلك الموضع. إذا عرفت هذا، فإن كان المصلي على دكان، ويمر الآخرُ أَمامَهُ تحت الدكان فلا شك أنه لم يمر في موضع سجوده حقيقة فلا يأثم على الرواية الأولى، وأما على الثانية فالمار تحت الدكان إن مرَّ في موضع النَّظر إذا نظر في موضع السجود، فحينئذ إن حاذى بعض أعضاء المار بعض أعضاء المصلي يأثم وإلا فلا. كذا في «البحر الرائق»). [١] قوله: موضع السجود؛ فإنّه لو كان المراد المعنى المشهور، لزم بإطلاقه لزوم الإثم لمن مرَّ أمام المصلي في المسجد مطلقاً، ولم يقل به أحد، وأيضاً على تقدير إرادته لا يعلم حكم الصحراء من المتن. [٢] قوله: الصغير؛ هو أقل من ستين ذراعاً، وقيل: من أربعين، وهو المختار كما في «جامع الرموز» عن «الجواهر». [٣] قوله: أمام المصلي؛ أي من موضع قدميه إلى حائط القبلة، وكذلك الحكم في الدار والبيت. [٤] قوله: كان؛ الضمير إلى المرور وإلى المصلّي. [٥] قوله: مكان واحد؛ قال في «رد المحتار»: «أي من حيث إنّه لم يجعل الفاصل فيه بقدر صفين مانعاً من الاقتداء، تنزيلاً له منزلة مكان واحد، بخلاف المسجد الكبير، فإنّه جُعِلَ فيه مانعاً، فكذا هاهنا يجعلُ ما بين يدي المصلي إلى حائط القبلة مكاناً واحداً بخلاف المسجد الكبير والصحراء، فإنّه لو جعل كذلك لزم الحرج على المارة، فاقتصر على موضع السجود». [٦] قوله: فعند بعض المشايخ؛ هذا هو الذي اختاره النَّسفي في «الكنز» وصححه في «الكافي»، وهو مختار صاحب «الهداية»، وشمس الأئمة السرخسي، وقاضي خان، وفي «المحيط»: هو أحسن؛ لأنَّ ذلك القدر أي من موضع قدمه إلى  عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية وإلا فلا. وعند البعض: الموضع الذي يقع عليه النظر إذا كان المصلي ناظراً في موضع سجوده له حكم موضع السجود، فيأثم بالمرور في ذلك الموضع. إذا عرفت هذا، فإن كان المصلي على دكان، ويمر الآخرُ أَمامَهُ تحت الدكان فلا شك أنه لم يمر في موضع سجوده حقيقة فلا يأثم على الرواية الأولى، وأما على الثانية فالمار تحت الدكان إن مرَّ في موضع النَّظر إذا نظر في موضع السجود، فحينئذ إن حاذى بعض أعضاء المار بعض أعضاء المصلي يأثم وإلا فلا. كذا في «البحر الرائق»). [١] قوله: موضع سجوده؛ قال في «البحر»: ذكر التمرتاشي أنّ الأصح أنه إن كان بحال لو صلى صلاة خاشع لا يقعُ بصره على المارّ، فلا يكره المرور، نحو أن يكون بصره في قيامه إلى موضع سجوده، وفي ركوعه إلى صدور قدميه، وفي سجوده إلى أرنبة أنفه، وفي قعوده إلى حجره، وفي سلامه إلى منكبيه. [٢] قوله: دكان؛ بضم الدال المهملة وتشديد الكاف: الحانوت، فارسي معرب. كذا في «الصحاح»، وفي حكمه كلّ مرتفع: كالسرير والسطح. [٣] قوله: بعض أعضاء المصلّي؛ قال في «جامع الرموز»: «محاذاة الأعضاء الأعضاء يستوي فيه جميع أعضاء المار، هو الصحيح كما في «التتمة»، وأعضاء المصلي
The book of prayer: What invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. [1] And in the interpretation of the place of prostration there is detail. Know that if the prayer is in a small mosque, passing in front of the worshipper wherever [it may be] necessitates sin, because the small mosque is one place, so the front of the worshipper wherever he is has the ruling of the place of prostration. And if it is in a large mosque or in the desert: according to some mashayikh: if he passes in the place of prostration, he sins, otherwise not. And according to others: the place that falls under his gaze, if the worshipper is looking at his place of prostration, has the ruling of the place of prostration, so he sins by passing in that place. If you know this, then if the worshipper is on a raised platform [dukkan], and another passes in front of him beneath the platform, there is no doubt that he did not pass in his place of prostration in reality, so he does not sin according to the first narration. As for the second, if the passer beneath the platform passes in the place of his gaze when he looks at the place of prostration, then if some limbs of the passer are opposite some limbs of the worshipper, he sins, otherwise not. This is in al-Bahr al-Raiq. [1] His saying: place of prostration; because if the well-known meaning was intended, its absolute application would necessitate sin for whoever passes in front of the worshipper in the mosque absolutely, and no one said that. Also, on the assumption of intending it, the ruling of the desert would not be known from the matn. [2] His saying: small; it is less than sixty cubits, and it is said: from forty, and it is the chosen [view] as in Jami' al-Rumuz from al-Jawahir. [3] His saying: in front of the worshipper; i.e. from the place of his feet to the qibla wall, and similarly the ruling in the house and dwelling. [4] His saying: is; the pronoun refers to the passing and the worshipper. [5] His saying: one place; said in Radd al-Muhtar: i.e. because the separation of two rows was not made a preventer of iqtida, considering it as one place, unlike the large mosque, where it was made a preventer. So here too, what is between the hands of the worshipper to the qibla wall is made one place, unlike the large mosque and desert, because if it were made so, it would cause hardship for passers-by, so it was limited to the place of prostration. [6] His saying: according to some mashayikh; this is the one chosen by al-Nasafi in al-Kanz and verified in al-Kafi, and it is the choice of the author of al-Hidayah, and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, and Qadikhan, and in al-Muhit: it is better; because that amount, i.e. from the place of his feet to [page 188] ... (continues similarly).
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Al-Wiqaya, expanding the ruling on passing in front of a praying person. The author defines the critical term 'place of prostration' (mawdi' al-sujud) and presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf among later scholars. The ruling distinguishes between a small mosque, where the entire area in front of the worshipper to the qibla wall is considered the place of prostration, and a large mosque or open desert, where only the immediate spot of prostration is considered. The first view (of some mashayikh) holds that passing only in the actual place of prostration is sinful. The second view extends the prohibited area to wherever the worshipper's gaze falls when looking at his place of prostration. The text then applies these rulings to a case where the worshipper is on a raised platform (dukkan) and a passerby walks underneath; on the first view, there is no sin since the passer is not in the place of prostration, but on the second view, he sins if his limbs are opposite the worshipper's limbs and he passes within the line of sight. The commentator also defines key terms like 'small mosque' (less than 60 cubits, or 40 as chosen) and 'in front of the worshipper' (from his feet to the qibla wall). The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the first view, as adopted by major Hanafi authorities like al-Hidayah and al-Kanz.

Key Terms
موضع السجود (mawdi' al-sujud): The place where the worshipper prostrates; in this context, it is defined as the area from the worshipper's feet to the qibla wall, and further specified based on mosque size and scholarly opinion.
المسجد الصغير (al-masjid al-saghir): A small mosque, defined as less than 60 cubits (or 40 in the chosen view). In such a mosque, the entire space in front of the worshipper is considered as the place of prostration.
الدكان (al-dukkan): A raised platform or shop; any elevated place like a bench or rooftop. The ruling addresses passing underneath such an elevation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the Hanafi school, if a person passes in front of a worshipper who is praying in a large mosque, where does the prohibition (sin) apply?
A) Anywhere in front of the worshipper within the mosque
B) Only within the worshipper's place of prostration (from feet to qibla wall)
C) Only within the worshipper's line of sight
D) There is no prohibition in a large mosque

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of passing before a worshipper is limited to the defined 'place of prostration', which in large mosques is only the immediate area from the worshipper's feet to the qibla wall, as per the mufta bihi view.
