Ruling on Squatting (Iq'a') in Prayer
Definition and ruling of the squatting posture (iq'a') in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iq'a', identify its two interpretations, and explain the evidence leading to its dislike.

Core Idea
Iq'a' (squatting) in prayer is disliked based on prohibitions in multiple hadith.

In the Hanafi school, the posture of iq'a' (squatting) during prayer is considered makruh (disliked). The commentator in 'Umdat al-Ri'aya cites several hadith collections that prohibit this posture and explains two slightly different interpretations, both of which are disliked. This lesson unpacks the definition, evidence, and Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is iq'a' and what is the hukm according to the text?
The text states both forms of iq'a' are "makruh".
2
Locate the dalil
What evidence is cited? The commentator mentions that the prohibition is found in Muslim, al-Tirmidhi, Ahmad, al-Bayhaqi, Ibn Majah, and others.
The hadith are not quoted verbatim but referenced.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the prohibition indicate the ruling? The prohibition in ahad hadith leads to dislike (makruh tanzihi), not haram, because the evidence is zanni.
Hanafi usul: ahad hadith can establish obligation or prohibition but the level depends on strength.
4
Note the ikhtilaf in definition
Two interpretations of iq'a' are given: one from al-Hidayah (sitting on heels with knees erect) and one from al-Karkhi (erecting feet as in sujud and placing buttocks on heels).
Both are ruled as disliked.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi
Which position is relied upon? The mufta bihi is that both forms of iq'a' are disliked; the preferred definition is from al-Hidayah.
No tarjih between the two interpretations is given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: واقِْعاؤُهُ، وهو القعود على اليتيه ناصباً ركبتيه، وافتراش ذراعيه. قوله: واقعاؤه: لورود النهي عنه عند مسلم والترمذي وأحمد والبيهقي وابن ماجه وغيرهم. قوله: وهو القعود... الخ: هذا التفسير هو الذي اختاره في الهداية، وفسره الكرخي بأن ينصب قدميه كما في السجود، ويضع إليتيه على عقبيه، وهو أيضاً مكروه، ذكره ابن الهمام في الفتح.
He said: 'And his iq'a' (squatting), which is sitting on the heels with knees erect, and spreading the forearms. He said: 'His iq'a': because the prohibition of it has come from Muslim, al-Tirmidhi, Ahmad, al-Bayhaqi, Ibn Majah, and others. He said: 'And it is the sitting... etc.': This interpretation is the one chosen in al-Hidayah, and al-Karkhi interpreted it as erecting the feet as in prostration and placing the buttocks on the heels, and it is also disliked; Ibn al-Humam mentioned it in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage discusses the disliked posture of iq'a' (squatting) during prayer. The commentator first defines iq'a' as sitting on the heels with knees erect. He then notes that a prohibition has been narrated in several hadith collections. Two interpretations of iq'a' are given: the one chosen in al-Hidayah (sitting on heels with knees up), and al-Karkhi's interpretation (erecting the feet like in sujud and sitting on the heels). Both are considered disliked. The commentator cites Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir for the second interpretation, confirming it is also makruh.

Key Terms
الإقعاء (al-iq'a'): Squatting; sitting on the heels with knees erect (or erecting feet like in sujud).
اليتيه (al-ityatayn): The buttocks; the part of the body on which one sits in iq'a'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Iq'a' in prayer is disliked, and both of its interpretations carry the same ruling.

Ruling on Spreading Forearms in Sujud for Men and Women
Ruling on spreading forearms during prostration for men and women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on spreading forearms in sujud for men and women and explain the evidence from hadith.

Core Idea
Spreading forearms in sujud is makruh tahrimi for men but not for women.

The posture during prostration (sujud) is a matter of fiqh. While some actions are recommended, others are discouraged. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the ruling on spreading forearms flat on the ground—a posture compared to a dog. The ruling differs between men and women, based on a prophetic prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being ruled
The passage defines 'iftirash al-dhira'ayn' as spreading the forearms flat on the ground during sujud, without raising them.
This is the opposite of 'mujafah' (raising the arms slightly).
2
Determine the ruling for men
The text explicitly states that for men, this action is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked), as mentioned by al-Bahr.
Makruh tahrimi means close to haram and requires avoidance.
3
Determine the ruling for women
The text concludes: 'هذا للرجل لا للمرأة' (this is for the man, not for the woman), indicating women are exempt from this dislike.
Women may spread their arms, and it is not disliked for them.
4
Identify the evidence
The ruling is based on a hadith narrated by Ahmad and other reliable sources (implicitly the famous hadith 'Do not spread your forearms like a dog').
The hadith is ahad, but its meaning is clear (qat'i al-dalala).
5
Note the gender distinction
The difference is due to women's need for modesty and covering; spreading arms helps conceal their body during sujud.
Other schools may apply the ruling to both genders, but the Hanafi school makes a distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وافتراش ذراعيه: بأن يبسطهما في حالة السجود، ولا يجافيها عن الأرض، وكراهته تحريماً ذكره في البحر؛ لورود النهي عنه عند أحمد وغيره من كتب الصحاح، وهذا للرجل لا للمرأة.
He said: 'And spreading his forearms': that he spreads them in the state of prostration and does not lift them away from the ground, and its dislike is tahrimi, mentioned in al-Bahr; due to the prohibition of it coming from Ahmad and other reliable books, and this is for the man, not for the woman.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage addresses the posture of spreading the forearms flat on the ground during sujud (prostration). The commentator defines it as placing the arms flat without raising them. This action is makruh tahrimi for men, based on the hadith prohibition narrated by Ahmad and others. However, this ruling applies specifically to men; women are exempted and may spread their arms to maintain modesty. The source cited is al-Bahr (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq).

Key Terms
افتراش الذراعين (iftirash al-dhira'ayn): Spreading the forearms flat on the ground during prostration, as a dog does.
المجافاة (al-mujafah): Raising the arms away from the ground; the opposite of spreading. For men, it is required to raise the arms slightly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, for whom is spreading forearms in sujud makruh tahrimi?
A) Both men and women
B) Men only
C) Women only
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Spreading forearms flat in sujud is makruh tahrimi for men only; women are exempt and may do so without dislike.

Sitting Cross-Legged in Prayer: Ruling and Excuse
Ruling on sitting cross-legged in prayer with excuse exemption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sitting cross-legged in prayer and distinguish between the default makruh tanzihi and the exception for excuses.

Core Idea
Sitting cross-legged in prayer is lightly disliked; necessity makes it permissible.

This lesson examines a short but instructive passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifying the ruling on tarbbu' (sitting cross-legged) during salah. While the sunnah posture is iftirash, leaving it for tarbbu' incurs a tanzihi dislike. However, when an excuse exists—such as injury or inability—the ruling flips to permissible. We'll unpack the proof from the practice of Ibn 'Umar and the legal maxim at work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is sitting cross-legged (tarbbu') during prayer. The text states it is makruh tanzihi, i.e., lightly disliked.
2
Define the sunnah posture
The sunnah sitting (al-qa'dah al-masnunah) is iftirash: spreading the left foot and sitting on it, with the right foot erect. Leaving this for tarbbu' is disliked.
3
Locate the exception
The text says 'and the case of an excuse is excepted'. Necessities permit what is normally disliked or prohibited.
The maxim 'al-ḍarūrāt tubīḥu al-maḥẓūrāt' is invoked.
4
Examine the proof
The text cites the report that Ibn 'Umar used to sit cross-legged, which is evidence that when there is a genuine excuse, it is permissible. Also, the general maxim of necessity applies.
The proof is from practice (sunnah ahad) and legal maxim.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools may consider it permissible unconditionally; the Hanafi position is that following the sunnah is recommended, but deviation is only disliked, not sinful.
Cross-madhhab: mukhtalaf fih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وتربعه: كراهة تنزيهية، كما في الدر المختار؛ لأن فيه ترك القعدة المسنونة، وهي افتراش اليسرى والجلوس عليها، ونصب اليمنى، وحالة العذر مستثناة؛ لأن الضرورات تبيح المحظورات، وقد ورد أن ابن عمر كان يتربع.
He said: 'And his sitting cross-legged': it is a tanzihi dislike, as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar; because it involves leaving the sunnah sitting, which is spreading the left foot and sitting on it, and erecting the right foot, and the case of an excuse is excepted; because necessities permit prohibitions, and it has been reported that Ibn 'Umar used to sit cross-legged.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage addresses sitting cross-legged (tarbbu') during prayer, which is makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked) because it abandons the sunnah sitting posture known as iftirash (spreading the left foot and sitting on it, with the right foot erect). However, if there is an excuse (e.g., injury or inability to sit in the sunnah manner), it is permitted due to the legal maxim 'necessities permit prohibited things'. The commentator cites the example of Ibn 'Umar who sat cross-legged, presumably due to an excuse. The source is al-Durr al-Mukhtar.

Key Terms
التربع (al-tarbbu'): Sitting cross-legged during prayer, as opposed to the recommended iftirash posture.
القعدة المسنونة (al-qa'dah al-masnunah): The sunnah sitting posture in prayer: spreading the left foot and sitting on it, with the right foot erect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on sitting cross-legged in prayer without an excuse according to the Hanafi school?
A) Haram
B) Makruh tahrimi
C) Makruh tanzihi
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Sitting cross-legged in prayer is makruh tanzihi unless there is an excuse; the Hanafi school balances adherence to sunnah with flexibility for genuine need.

Ruling on Iqa' (Sitting on Buttocks) in Prayer
Ruling on iqa' with two interpretations and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on iqa' in prayer, identify the evidence, and distinguish between the two Hanafi interpretations.

Core Idea
Iqa' is disliked because the Prophet (saw) prohibited it; two forms are both makruh.

In this lesson, we examine the first of three disliked postures in prayer: iqa', or sitting on the buttocks with knees erect. The passage cites prohibitory hadith and presents two interpretive definitions from early Hanafi authorities. Understanding this ruling helps you refine your prayer posture to avoid disliked elements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the posture and its ruling
The passage defines iqa' as sitting on the buttocks with knees erect. The ruling is 'makruh' (disliked).
The term 'iqa' is often confused with sitting on the heels; the definition here is the one chosen in al-Hidayah.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a prohibitory hadith reported by Muslim, Tirmidhi, Ahmad, Bayhaqi, and Ibn Majah.
The exact wording is not quoted, but the presence of nahy (prohibition) is sufficient for karahah in Hanafi usul.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Karkhi interprets iqa' differently: erecting the feet as in sujud and placing the buttocks on the heels. Both forms are disliked.
Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath mentions that al-Karkhi's interpretation is also makruh.
4
Apply the ruling to practice
In your prayer, avoid sitting with knees drawn up or on your heels with feet erect. The sunnah is to sit on the left foot with the right foot erect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واقعاؤه، وافتراش ذراعيه، وتربعه بلا عذر
[١] (وإقعاؤه) ، وهو القعود على اليتيه ناصباً ركبتيه، وافتراش ذراعيه"، [؟] [١] قوله : واقعاؤه ؛ لورود النهي عنه عند مسلم والترمذي وأحمد والبَيْهَقِي وابن ماجة وغيرهم.
[٢]قوله : وهو القعود... الخ ؛ هذا التفسير هو الذي اختاره في «الهداية»، وفسره الكرخي بأن ينصب قدميه كما في السجود، ويضع إليتيه على عقبيه، وهو أيضاً مكروه، ذكره ابن الهمام في «الفتح».
[٣] قوله : وافتراش ذراعيه ؛ بأن يبسطهما في حالةِ السجود، ولا يجافيها عن الأرض، وكراهته تحريماً ذكره في «البحر» ؛ لورود النهي عنه عند أحمد وغيره من كتب الصحاح، وهذا للرجل لا للمرأة.
[٤]قوله : وتربعه ؛ كراهة تنزيهية، كما في «الدر المختار»؛ لأن فيه ترك القعدة المسنونة، وهي افتراش اليسرى والجلوس عليها، ونصب اليمني، وحالة العذر مستثناة؛ لأنَّ الضرورات تبيحُ المحظورات، وقد ورد أن ابن عمر كان يتربع، ويعلله بالوجع في رجله، وينهى غيره عنه، أخرجه مالك وغيره.
And his iqa', and spreading his forearms, and sitting cross-legged without excuse. [1] (And his iqa'): it is sitting on the buttocks with knees erect, and spreading of forearms. [1] His statement: 'and his iqa'' – because prohibition is reported from Muslim, Tirmidhi, Ahmad, Bayhaqi, Ibn Majah and others. [2] His statement: 'and it is sitting... etc.': this interpretation is the one chosen in al-Hidayah, and al-Karkhi interpreted it as erecting the feet as in prostration and placing the buttocks on the heels – that too is disliked, mentioned by Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath. [3] His statement: 'and spreading his forearms': meaning he spreads them in the state of prostration and does not raise them from the ground; its dislike is tahrimi, mentioned in al-Bahr – due to prohibition reported by Ahmad and others from authentic books, and this applies to men not women. [4] His statement: 'and sitting cross-legged': dislike of tanzih, as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar – because it abandons the sunnah sitting posture, which is spreading the left foot and sitting on it, and erecting the right; the excuse case is excepted because necessities permit prohibited things; and it is reported that Ibn Umar used to sit cross-legged, excusing it by pain in his leg, and forbade others from it, recorded by Malik and others.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof on three disliked postures in prayer: iqa', iftirash al-dhira'ayn, and tarbbu'. The author lists these as part of the matn from al-Wiqayah, then expands with proofs and details. For iqa' (sitting on the heels with knees erect), the ruling is general karahah (dislike), based on prohibitory hadith from several sources. The commentary notes two interpretations: the preferred one from al-Hidayah (sitting on buttocks with knees up) and al-Karkhi's (feet erect like in sujud). Both are disliked. For iftirash al-dhira'ayn (spreading forearms flat on the ground in sujud), the karahah is tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) for men, supported by a hadith warning against imitating a dog. For tarbbu' (sitting cross-legged without excuse), the karahah is tanzihi (lightly disliked) because it abandons the sunnah posture (tawarruk style). Exceptions are allowed for genuine excuses like leg pain, as narrated from Ibn Umar. The proofs are all ahadith, so the authority lies in the later Hanafi tradition (fatawa mutakhkhirin).

Key Terms
إقعاء (iqā'): Sitting on the buttocks with knees erect, or (per Karkhi) erecting feet as in sujud and sitting on heels.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the evidence for the Hanafi ruling that iqa' is makruh?
A) Ijma of the Companions
B) A prohibitory hadith reported by several narrators
C) A verse from the Quran
D) Qiyas on prohibition of imitating dogs

Key Takeaway
Iqa' is any sitting posture that deviates from the sunnah by raising the knees or erecting the feet on the heels, and it is disliked (makruh).

Ruling on Iftirash al-Dhira'ayn (Spreading Forearms) in Sujud
Ruling on iftirash al-dhira'ayn with hadith evidence and gender distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on iftirash al-dhira'ayn, distinguish the gender-specific application, and identify the evidence.

Core Idea
Spreading forearms flat in sujud is prohibitively disliked for men, but not for women.

The second disliked posture is iftirash al-dhira'ayn, or spreading the forearms flat on the ground during prostration. The passage warns that this is makruh tahrimi for men, based on a hadith that prohibits imitating a dog. Women are exempted. This lesson explains the ruling and its basis.

Guided Path
1
Define the posture
Iftirash al-dhira'ayn means to spread both forearms flat on the ground in sujud without raising them.
This is contrasted with the sunnah of raising the forearms and keeping them off the ground (mujafah).
2
Identify the ruling
The passage states it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) for men.
The karahah is tahrimi, meaning it is closer to haram and should be avoided.
3
Locate the dalil
The evidence is a hadith reported by Ahmad and other authentic collections that forbids this posture.
The hadith is understood to prohibit imitating a dog, which spreads its forelegs.
4
Note the gender exception
The ruling applies only to men; women are not disliked for doing this because they are commanded to be more constricted in prayer.
This is a common fiqh principle: women are encouraged to keep limbs close together.

Ruling on Tarbbu' (Sitting Cross-Legged) in Prayer
Ruling on tarbbu' with exception for excuses and hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on tarbbu', identify the exception for excuses, and cite the evidence from the practice of Ibn Umar.

Core Idea
Sitting cross-legged in prayer is lightly disliked unless there is an excuse like leg pain.

The third disliked posture is tarbbu' or sitting cross-legged during the prayer. The passage explains that this is makruh tanzihi because it abandons the sunnah sitting posture (muftarishan). However, an excuse such as leg pain makes it permissible, as demonstrated by Ibn Umar. This lesson covers the ruling and its nuance.

Guided Path
1
Define tarbbu'
Tarbbu' means sitting cross-legged, crossing one leg over the other.
This is opposed to the sunnah posture of sitting on the left foot and erecting the right.
2
Identify the ruling
The ruling is makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked) when done without an excuse.
Tanzihi means it is better to avoid, but not sinful.
3
Note the exception
If a person has an excuse (e.g., leg pain, inability to sit muftarishan), then tarbbu' is permissible and not disliked.
The principle 'al-darurat tubihu al-mahzurat' (necessities permit prohibitions) applies.
4
Locate the evidence
Ibn Umar used to sit cross-legged due to pain in his leg and forbade others from doing so without excuse. This is recorded by Malik in the Muwatta.
This shows that even a Companion distinguished between excused and unexcused tarbbu'.

Source Text

Imam Standing in the Mihrab or on a Raised Platform
Ruling on imam's placement relative to congregation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the imam standing alone in the mihrab or on a raised platform, identify the two justifications for dislike, and apply the exception (standing in mosque and prostrating in mihrab is not disliked).

Core Idea
An imam standing alone in the mihrab or on a high platform is disliked to avoid imitation and confusion, but using the mihrab only for sujud is fine.

This lesson examines a subtle fiqh ruling: when is it disliked for an imam to stand in the mihrab or on a raised platform? The classical texts distinguish between standing fully inside the mihrab (disliked) and using the mihrab only for prostration (not disliked). For platforms, the height matters. Let's explore the reasoning and proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First: the imam stands in the mihrab (taq). Second: the imam stands on a raised platform (dukkan). The text also mentions the imam standing on the ground while congregation on platform.
2
Distinguish the two cases within the mihrab
Scenario A: imam stands in the mosque (in front of mihrab) and prostrates inside the mihrab – not disliked by consensus. Scenario B: imam stands entirely inside the mihrab alone – this is disliked.
3
Understand the two justifications for dislike
First: tashabbuh (imitation) of non-Muslims who distinguish their leader by place. Second: possible confusion for those to the imam's right and left about his state. On the first reason, it is disliked absolutely; on the second, it is not disliked if visibility is good.
4
Analyze the raised platform case
The disliked height is debated: some say a man's height, some say enough to create distinction, but the majority prefer a cubit (like a sutrah). The evidence includes a hadith from Sunan Abi Dawud and the prohibition of imitating non-Muslims.
5
Apply the ruling to examples
If a platform is one cubit or less, it is not disliked. If higher, it is disliked. The same principle applies to other elevations like a minbar or stage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيام الإمام في طاق المسجد) : أي في المحراب، بأن يكون المحراب كبيراً، فيقوم فيه وحده، أو على دكان" أو على الأرض وحـده) : أي يقــوم الإمـام عـلـى الأرض والقوم على الدكان.
[١] قوله : وقيام الإمام....الخ ؛ هاهنا صورتان :
الأولى : أن يقوم في المسجد ويسجد في المحراب، وهذا لا كراهة فيه اتفاقاً، كما في «البناية». والثانية : أن يقوم فيه، وهي التي صرحوا بكراهتها، وعللوا الكراهة بوجهين : أحدهما : التشبه بأهل الكتاب في امتياز الإمام عن القوم بمكان.
والآخر: أن يشتبه حاله على من يمينه ويساره.
فعلى الأول يكره مطلقاً.
وعلى الثاني لا يكره إذا لم يخف حاله على أصحاب الجهتين.
[٢]قوله : أو على دكان...الخ ؛ أي على شيءٍ مرتفع كالدكان ونحوه قدر القامة أو قدر ما يقع به الامتياز، أو قدر ذراع كالسترة، فيه أقوال، والمختار عند الجمهور هو الأخير، كما في «الفتح»، وقد وردَ النهي عنه في سنن أبي داود» وغيره ؛ ولأنَّ فيها تشبهاً بأهل الكتاب، وقد نهينا عنه.
And the imam's standing in the arch of the mosque: i.e., in the mihrab, such that the mihrab is large and he stands in it alone, or on a platform, or on the ground alone: i.e., the imam stands on the ground and the people on the platform. [1] His statement: 'and the imam's standing... etc.': here are two scenarios: First: that he stands in the mosque and prostrates in the mihrab – there is no dislike in this according to agreed opinion, as in al-Binayah. Second: that he stands inside it (the mihrab) – this is what they declared disliked, and they justify the dislike on two grounds: One: imitation of the People of the Book in distinguishing the imam from the congregation by place. The other: that his condition may be hidden from those to his right and left. On the first ground, it is disliked absolutely. On the second, it is not disliked if his condition is not hidden from those on either side. [2] His statement: 'or on a platform... etc.': i.e., on something elevated like a platform or similar, of the height of a man, or enough to create distinction, or a cubit like a sutrah – there are opinions; the preferred view according to the majority is the latter, as in al-Fath, and prohibition is reported in Sunan Abi Dawud and others, and because it involves imitation of the People of the Book, and we have been forbidden from that.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This is a ruling_with_proof on the imam's placement in relation to the congregation. The matn discusses three cases: (1) imam standing in a large mihrab (taq), (2) on a raised platform (dakkan), or (3) on the ground while the congregation is on a platform. The commentary distinguishes two scenarios for the mihrab: if the imam stands inside the mosque and prostrates in the mihrab, it's not disliked by consensus. But if he stands entirely within the mihrab alone, it is disliked for two reasons: imitation of non-Muslims and possible confusion for those to his sides. On the first reason, it's always disliked; on the second, it's excused if visibility is clear. For the platform, the disliked height is a matter of ijtihad, with the majority viewing a cubit (like a sutrah) as the maximum. Proofs include hadith from Abu Dawud and the principle of avoiding imitation of non-Muslims. The ruling is from later Hanafi fatawa.

Key Terms
طاق المسجد (ṭāq al-masjid): The arch of the mosque, i.e., the mihrab or niche indicating qibla.
دكان (dukkān): A raised platform or bench in the mosque.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it NOT disliked for an imam to stand in the mihrab?
A) When he stands alone inside the mihrab
B) When he stands in the mosque and prostrates in the mihrab
C) When he stands on a high platform
D) When he stands on the ground and congregation on a platform

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school discourages an imam standing alone in the mihrab or on a high platform to avoid imitation of non-Muslims and confusion, but low elevations like a sutrah are permissible.

Ruling on Imam Standing in the Mihrab (Niche)
Explaining the ruling and reasons for dislike of imam standing in the mihrab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the imam standing in the mihrab and explain the two reasons for dislike.

Core Idea
Standing in the mihrab is disliked only if it mimics Ahl al-Kitab or confuses the congregation.

Prayer behind an imam is a communal act, and the imam's position affects the congregation's ability to follow. This lesson examines the Hanafi view on the imam standing inside the mihrab (niche) – a practice often seen in mosques. The passage distinguishes between two scenarios and clarifies when the act is disliked and when it is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two images
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) the imam stands in the mosque and prostrates in the mihrab, and (2) the imam stands inside the mihrab itself.
The first image has consensus on no dislike; the second is the disputed case.
2
Extract the ruling on the second image
For the second image, the scholars explicitly state dislike (karahah). Two reasons are given: resemblance to Ahl al-Kitab and potential confusion for the followers.
The ruling is makruh tanzih, not haram.
3
Analyze the condition
The dislike is not absolute; it depends on whether confusion is feared. If the imam's actions are clearly visible to those on both sides, the second reason is negated.
Thus the ruling is conditional: disliked if confusion is feared, otherwise not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها. وقيام الإمام في طاق المسجد، أو على دكان أو على الأرض وحده. [1] قوله : وقيام الإمام....الخ ؛ هاهنا صورتان : الأولى : أن يقوم في المسجد ويسجد في المحراب، وهذا لا كراهة فيه اتفاقاً، كما في «البناية». والثانية : أن يقوم فيه، وهي التي صرحوا بكراهتها، وعللوا الكراهة بوجهين : أحدهما : التشبه بأهل الكتاب في امتياز الإمام عن القوم بمكان. والآخر: أن يشتبه حاله على من يمينه ويساره. فعلى الأول يكره مطلقاً. وعلى الثاني لا يكره إذا لم يخف حاله على أصحاب الجهتين. [٢] قوله : أو على دكان...الخ ؛ أي على شيءٍ مرتفع كالدكان ونحوه قدر القامة أو قدر ما يقع به الامتياز، أو قدر ذراع كالسترة، فيه أقوال، والمختار عند الجمهور هو الأخير، كما في «الفتح»، وقد وردَ النهي عنه في سنن أبي داود وغيره ؛ ولأنَّ فيها تشبهاً بأهل الكتاب، وقد نهينا عنه. وأما العكس وهو أن يكون الإمام وحده أسفل فكراهته لأن فيه ازدراء بالإمام ، وتكريمه مطلوب شرعاً.
Book of Prayer: What nullifies prayer and what is disliked in it. And the imam standing in a niche of the mosque, or on a platform, or on the ground alone. [1] His saying: 'and the imam standing... etc.' Here there are two images: First: that he stands in the mosque and prostrates in the niche, and there is no dislike in this by agreement, as in al-Binaya. Second: that he stands in it, and they explicitly stated its dislike, and they explained the dislike with two aspects: one: resemblance to the People of the Book in distinguishing the imam from the people by place. The other: that his condition may be confused for those on his right and left. According to the first, it is disliked absolutely. According to the second, it is not disliked if his condition is not hidden from those on both sides. [2] His saying: 'or on a platform... etc.' i.e., on something elevated like a platform and the like, the height of a person or the extent by which distinction occurs, or a cubit like a sutra; there are sayings, and the chosen according to the majority is the last, as in al-Fath. And the prohibition has been reported in Sunan Abi Dawud and others; because it resembles the People of the Book, and we have been prohibited from it. And as for the opposite, that the imam alone be below, its dislike is because it contains belittlement of the imam, and honoring him is required legally.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage from the sharh explains the ruling of the imam's position in prayer. It addresses two main scenarios: (1) the imam standing in the mihrab (niche) and (2) the imam standing on a raised platform (dakkan). For the first, there are two sub-cases: if the imam stands in the mosque and prostrates inside the mihrab, there is no dislike by consensus. But if he stands inside the mihrab itself (i.e., stands in the niche alone), it is disliked for two reasons: it resembles the practice of the People of the Book who distinguish their leader, and it may cause confusion for those praying behind him. However, if there is no fear of confusion (e.g., the niche is wide enough that his actions are visible), it is not disliked. For the second scenario of standing on a platform, the dislikedness is established by hadith reports and analogical reasoning based on resemblance to non-Muslim practices. The preferred height for such elevation is about a cubit (dhira'), as chosen by the majority. Finally, the opposite case—where the imam is lower than the congregation—is also disliked because it shows disrespect to the imam, whose honor is prescribed by the Shari'ah.

Key Terms
طاق المسجد (taq al-masjid): The niche (mihrab) in the mosque where the imam stands
الكراهة (al-karahah): Dislike; a ruling between permissible and prohibited

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the imam standing alone in the mihrab?
A) It is prohibited
B) It is disliked absolutely
C) It is disliked if it causes confusion for the followers, otherwise not
D) It is permissible

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between the imam standing in the mihrab leading prayer: if his actions are visible, it is not disliked; otherwise it is makruh tanzih.

Ruling on Imam Standing on a Raised Platform (Dakkan)
Explaining the ruling and reasons for dislike of imam standing on a platform in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the imam leading from a raised platform, identify the hadith evidence, and explain the chosen height.

Core Idea
Leading from a platform is disliked due to resembling Ahl al-Kitab and was prohibited in hadith; the minimal height is a cubit.

In some mosques, the imam leads prayer from a slightly elevated platform. This lesson examines the Hanafi position on this practice, drawing on hadith and the reasoning of tashabbuh. The passage also addresses the opposite scenario where the imam is lower than the congregation and provides the preferred height for any elevation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the platform scenario
The passage discusses the imam standing on something elevated like a dakkan (platform). The height is debated: a full height, a distinguishing height, or a cubit.
The majority choose a cubit as the maximum.
2
Note the hadith evidence
A prohibition (nahy) is reported in Sunan Abi Dawud and others regarding the imam's elevation. This serves as the primary dalil.
The prohibition is interpreted as karahah, not haram, due to other evidence.
3
Understand the reason (illah)
The reason for the ruling is resemblance to the People of the Book, which is prohibited. The prohibition is applied to the platform case via qiyas.
The same rationale applies as in the mihrab case.
4
Apply the ruling to the opposite case
The passage also mentions that if the imam is alone below the congregation, it is disliked because it belittles the imam. This is an independent ruling based on the requirement to honor the imam.
This shows that the dislikedness is not one-sided.

Standing Alone Behind a Row with a Gap
Ruling on standing alone behind a row when a gap exists

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on standing alone behind a row with a gap and identify the preferred action.

Core Idea
If a gap exists in the front row, standing alone behind is disliked; filling the gap by pulling someone is better.

This passage addresses a common scenario: a worshipper finds a gap in the front row but chooses to pray alone behind the row. The hadith cited, 'Complete the front row, then the one next to it...' indicates that completing the front before moving to the back is ideal. Therefore, standing alone behind when a gap exists is disliked (makruh tanzih). However, if one cannot find a gap or pulling someone is not feasible, standing alone is permissible but pulling someone from the row to fill the gap is the better practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is standing alone behind a row when a gap exists in the front row. The ruling is makruh tanzih (disliked).
Not haram or invalid; the prayer is valid but the act is discouraged.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'Complete the front row, then the one next to it, and whatever deficiency remains let it be in the last row' (Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i).
The command to complete the front row first implies that standing behind before completion is contrary to the sunnah.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'atimmū' (complete) indicates obligation or at least strong recommendation. The implication is that any deficiency in the front should be filled before moving to the back.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-nadb here due to other evidence.
4
Identify the exception
If there is no gap in the front row, standing alone behind is not disliked (la ba'sa bihi). However, pulling someone from the row is still better.
The exception is when the front row is completely filled with no space.
5
State the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position: It is disliked to stand alone behind when a gap exists; if no gap, it is permissible but pulling someone is preferable.
Source: al-Ghunyiah and others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقيام خلف صف وجد فيه فرجة وصورة. [١]قوله : والقيام...الخ ؛ وذلك لحديث: «أتموا الصف المقدم، ثم الذي بليه، فما كان من نقص فليكن في الصف المؤخّر»، أخرجه أبو داود والنسائي، وهو يفيد كراهة القيام في المؤخّر قبل إتمام المقدّم، وإن لم يكن وحده فكراهة قيامه وحده أولى : فإن لم يجد في الصف فرجة فقام منفرداً خلفه، فلا بأس به، والأولى أن يجذب واحداً منه. كذا في «الغُنية»، وغيره. [۲] قوله : وصورة ... الخ ؛ أي تكره الصلاة إذا كانت الصورة قدام المصلي أو عنى جنبه الأيمن أو الأيسر أو فوق رأسه في السقف، أو معلقة على السقف، أو في الستر.
And standing behind a row in which one finds a gap and an image. [1] His saying: 'and standing... etc.' due to the hadith: 'Complete the front row, then the one next to it, and whatever deficiency remains let it be in the last row.' Reported by Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i. It indicates the dislikedness of standing in the back before completing the front. Even if not alone, the dislikedness of standing alone is greater. If one does not find a gap in the row and stands alone behind it, there is no harm, but it is better to pull one person from it. This is in al-Ghunyiah and others. [2] His saying: 'and an image... etc.' i.e., the prayer is disliked if the image is in front of the worshipper or on his right or left side, or above his head on the ceiling, or hanging on the ceiling, or in the curtain.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of disliked actions in prayer. The first part addresses the scenario where a person finds a gap in a row but chooses to stand alone behind the row. The hadith emphasizing completing the front rows first implies that it is disliked to stand in the back before the front is filled. If the gap is present, it is better to fill it; but if one cannot (e.g., the gap is too narrow), it is permissible to stand alone behind, though pulling someone from the row to fill the gap is better. The second part addresses the presence of images (surah) during prayer: it is disliked to have any picture or statue in front, beside, or above the worshipper, whether on the ceiling, hanging, or in a curtain. This is based on the principle of avoiding distraction and resemblance to idol worship.

Key Terms
الفرجة (al-furjah): A gap or empty space in the prayer row

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person finds a gap in the front row but decides to stand alone behind, what is the ruling?
A) Prayer is invalid
B) Prayer is valid but disliked
C) Prayer is valid and recommended
D) It is prohibited to stand alone

Key Takeaway
Standing alone behind a row when a gap exists is disliked (makruh tanzih); filling the gap is better.

Presence of Images During Prayer
Ruling on images during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions where images are disliked during prayer and state the ruling.

Core Idea
Having an image in front, beside, above, or on the curtain during prayer is disliked.

This passage addresses the presence of images (pictures or statues) in the prayer area. According to the Hanafi school, it is makruh tanzih to pray while an image is in front of the worshipper, on the right or left, above the head (on the ceiling), hanging, or on a curtain. The ruling aims to avoid distraction and resemblance to acts of worship directed towards images.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is praying while an image is present in certain locations relative to the worshipper. The ruling is makruh tanzih.
The prayer itself remains valid; it is not invalidated.
2
Determine the prohibited positions
The image is disliked if it is in front, to the right side, to the left side, above the head (on ceiling), hanging from ceiling, or on a curtain.
These are the specific locations mentioned in the text.
3
Understand the rationale
The rationale is to avoid distraction and any resemblance to image worship. The hadiths about angels not entering houses with images support this.
No explicit hadith cited here, but it is based on general principles.
4
Note the scope
The dislikedness applies only if the image is clearly visible or likely to distract. If the image is behind the worshipper or covered, it may not be disliked.
The text limits to the specified directions.

Standing Alone Behind a Row When a Gap Exists
Ruling with proof on the dislike of standing behind a row when a gap exists

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on standing alone behind a row when a gap exists and explain the evidence from the hadith.

Core Idea
Standing alone behind a row when a gap exists is makruh tanzih, but the prayer remains valid.

In congregational prayer, the arrangement of rows carries specific rulings. This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya clarifies what happens when a worshipper finds a gap in the front row and decides to stand alone behind it. Understanding this ruling prevents unnecessary nullification of prayer and ensures proper etiquette.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is standing alone behind a row when a gap exists in the front row. The Hanafi ruling is that it is makruh tanzih (disliked but not sinful).
If there is no gap, standing alone is permissible but pulling someone is better.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'Complete the front row, then the one after it, and whatever deficiency remains let it be in the last row.' This is from Sunan Abi Dawud and al-Nasa'i.
The imperative 'Ammu' is used, but Hanafis interpret it as a recommendation, not an obligation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith commands completing front rows and placing deficiency in the last row. The Hanafi deduction is that leaving a gap in a front row is disliked, but the prayer is valid because the deficiency is merely discouraged, not prohibited.
The proof is zanni in both transmission and meaning, leading to a ruling of dislike rather than prohibition.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools consider it obligatory to fill gaps in front rows, deeming the prayer invalid if one stands alone. The Hanafi school holds it merely disliked.
The difference stems from interpreting the imperative in the hadith.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position relies on the principle that commands (amr) indicate recommendation unless there is evidence of obligation. The second part of the hadith ('let the deficiency be in the last row') shows that incomplete front rows are permissible but less virtuous.
Thus, the mufta bihi is karahah tanzih, and the prayer is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقيام خلف صف وجد فيه فرجة
(1)
وأما العكس وهو أن يكون الإمام وحده أسفل فكراهته لأن فيه ازدراء بالإمام، وتكريمه مطلوب شرعاً. [١] قوله: والقيام...الخ؛ وذلك لحديث: «أتموا الصف المقدم، ثم الذي بليه، فما كان من نقص فليكن في الصف المؤخّر»، أخرجه أبو داود والنسائي، وهو يفيد كراهة القيام في المؤخّر قبل إتمام المقدّم، وإن لم يكن وحده فكراهة قيامه وحده أولى: فإن لم يجد في الصف فرجة فقام منفرداً خلفه، فلا بأس به، والأولى أن يجذب واحداً منه. كذا في «الغُنية»، وغيره.
And standing behind a row in which one finds a gap. (1) As for the opposite, that the imam alone is lower, it is disliked because it involves disrespect to the imam, and honoring him is legally required. [1] His saying: 'And standing...etc.'; that is due to the hadith: 'Complete the front row, then the one after it, and whatever deficiency remains let it be in the last row' – reported by Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i. This indicates the dislike of standing in the rear before the front row is completed. And if he is not alone, the dislike of his standing alone is greater. If he finds no gap in the row and stands alone behind it, there is no harm, but it is better to pull one person from the row to join him. Thus in al-Ghunya and others.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about the etiquette of rows in congregational prayer. The matn states that it is disliked to stand behind a row when a gap exists in the row ahead. The commentator first mentions an opposite case: if the imam stands alone at a lower level, that is also disliked because it shows disrespect. Then he explains that the ruling is based on the hadith commanding completion of front rows. The hadith is used as proof that leaving the front rows incomplete is disliked, and the deficiency should be in the last rows. The commentator clarifies that if a person finds no gap and stands alone behind the row, the prayer is valid (no harm), but it is better to pull someone from the row to stand next to him. This follows the Hanafi view that such standing is makruh tanzih (disliked but not sinful) and does not invalidate the prayer.

Key Terms
فرجة (farjah): A gap or break in the row of worshippers.
كريحة (karāhah): Dislike; a legal category between mubah and haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) His prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) It is permissible without any dislike.
C) It is makruh (disliked) but the prayer is valid.
D) It is haram and the prayer is void.

Key Takeaway
Standing alone behind a row when a gap exists is makruh tanzih (disliked) but does not invalidate the prayer.

Images in Prayer: Positions of Dislike and the Exception of Humiliation
Ruling on images in prayer with explanation of exception and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions in which an image of a living being makes prayer makruh in the Hanafi school, and identify the positions where it is not disliked, citing the dalil and the principle of imtihan.

Core Idea
Prayer is disliked with a living image in front, side, or above; but not if behind or underfoot.

Images of living beings in the prayer area raise questions about validity and dislike. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the exact positions that trigger karahah and the exception based on humiliation (imtihan). Understanding this ruling helps you avoid unnecessary dislike while recognizing the underlying principle of avoiding resemblance to idol worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is praying (salah) while an image of a living being is present. The ruling is makruh tanzih (slightly disliked), not haram or invalid.
The ruling is limited to images of animate beings (dhi ruh).
2
Locate the positions of karahah
The text specifies four positions that trigger dislike: in front of the worshipper, to the right or left side, on the ceiling above, or hanging. These positions involve direct visibility or exaltation of the image.
Images placed behind or underfoot are excluded because they are not honored.
3
Apply the exception based on humiliation
If the image is on a carpet, cushion, or other item that is trampled or sat upon, it is considered humiliated (muhtan) and thus not disliked. The hadith exception for bedding supports this.
The principle: anything that imitates idol worship or honors the image causes karahah; otherwise not.
4
Trace the dalil and its implication
The primary proof is the hadith: 'Angels do not enter a house with a dog or an image.' The Hanafi school interprets this as indicating karahah, not tahrim. The exception in the Sahihayn for bedding allows humiliated images.
The hadith is ahad and zanni in thubut and dalalah, hence the ruling is makruh tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وصورة ... الخ ; أي تكره الصلاة إذا كانت الصورة قدام المصلي أو عنى جنبه الأيمن أو الأيسر أو فوق رأسه في السقف، أو معلقة على السقف، أو في الستر،

أمامه، أو بحذائه، أو في السقف أو معلّقة، أي صورة حيوان)، (أمامه ، أو بحذائه) : أي على أحد جنبيه ، (أو في السقف، أو معلقة)، فإن كانت خلفه، أو تحت قدميه لا يُكْرَه.

والأصل في هذا كل ما كان فيه تشبّه بعبادة الأوثان أو وجد فيه تعظيم الصورة تكره فيه الصلاة، وما لا فلا، وإن كان وضع الصورة في البيت ممنوعاً مطلقاً إلا في البساط والوسادة وغيرهما مما يمتهن ويهان، كذا ذكره شراح «الهداية». والأصل فيه حديث: «لا تدخل الملائكة بيتاً فيه كلب ولا صورة، أخرجه أبو داود والنسائي وابن ماجه وأحمد وابن حبان وغيرهم، والحديث مُخرج في الصحيحين بألفاظ متقاربة، وفيهما ما يدلّ على جوازها في الفراش ونحوه.

[١] قوله : صورة حيوان؛ لما كانت الصورة شاملة لصورة ذي روح وغير ذي روح، بخلاف التمثال، فإنّه مختص بذي روح كما في «المغرب»، وغيره، وكانت الكراهة مختصة بصورة ذي روح، احتاج الشارح له إلى تفسيره وتقييده.
His saying: 'And an image ... etc.' That is, prayer is disliked if the image is in front of the worshipper, or on his right or left side, or above his head on the ceiling, or hanging from the ceiling, or in the curtain. In front of him, or beside him, or on the ceiling, or hanging, meaning an image of an animal. (In front of him or beside him) meaning on one of his sides. (Or on the ceiling or hanging) so if it is behind him or under his feet, it is not disliked. The principle in this is: anything that involves imitation of idol worship or found in exaltation of the image, prayer is disliked in it; otherwise not. And although placing images in the house is absolutely prohibited except on carpets, cushions, and other things that are trampled and degraded, as mentioned by the commentators of al-Hidayah. The basis for this is the hadith: 'Angels do not enter a house in which there is a dog or an image' - reported by Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, Ahmad, Ibn Hibban, and others. The hadith is narrated in the Two Sahihs with similar wording, and in them it indicates permissibility on bedding and the like. [1] His saying: 'Image of an animal' - because the word 'surah' includes both living and non-living things, unlike 'tamthal' which is specific to living things as in al-Mughrib. And since the dislike is specific to the image of a living being, the commentator needed to interpret and restrict it.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling about images (surah) in the prayer area. The matn states that prayer is disliked if an image is placed in specific positions: in front, to the sides, or above the worshipper. The commentator clarifies that this applies to images of living beings (like animals or humans). The underlying principle is that such images cause resemblance to idol worship and exaltation of the image, which is disliked in prayer. However, if the image is behind or under the feet, it is not disliked because it is not directly in view or is trampled. The hadith about angels not entering a house with an image is cited as proof, but the exception for bedding shows that images that are humiliated (like on carpets) are allowed. The commentator also distinguishes between 'surah' (image) and 'tamthal' (statue), noting that the dislike applies only to living beings. This is a standard Hanafi ruling: it is makruh tanzih to pray with an image of a living being in direct sight, but not if it is behind or underfoot.

Key Terms
صورة (surah): Image or picture; includes both living and non-living things, but the ruling concerns images of living beings.
تمثال (tamthal): Statue or figure; specifically of a living being, as distinguished from surah.
إمتهان (imtihān): Being humiliated or degraded, as in images on carpets or cushions that are stepped on.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits prayer with images of living beings provided they are behind or underfoot, based on the principle of humiliation (imtihan).

Praying with Uncovered Head Out of Laziness
Ruling on head covering in prayer based on motive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying with uncovered head and distinguish between laziness and incapacity as motives.

Core Idea
Leaving the head uncovered in prayer is disliked when due to laziness, not when due to inability.

This lesson clarifies a common point of confusion: is covering the head obligatory in prayer? The Hanafi school teaches that it is a recommended custom, not a pillar. However, deliberately omitting it out of laziness or negligence is discouraged. The key is the intention behind the action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being ruled upon
The act is 'praying with uncovered head' (حاسر الرأس). The passage specifies the motive: laziness (كسل) or negligence (تهاون).
The act itself is not inherently prohibited; the ruling depends on the reason.
2
Distinguish laziness from incapacity
Laziness (كسل) is abandoning the act due to lack of desire or considering it unimportant. Incapacity (عجز) is being unable to perform it. The passage explicitly distinguishes these.
Incapacity removes the dislike; laziness confirms it.
3
Determine the legal ruling (hukm)
Based on the distinction, the ruling is 'makruh tanzih' (disliked) when done out of laziness. If done out of incapacity, it is not disliked.
Makruh tanzih means it is best to avoid, but not sinful.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The dislike arises from showing negligence in prayer by omitting a customary act of respect. The intention (laziness vs. inability) changes the ruling.
The principle is that neglect in worship is blameworthy.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
If a person finds it inconvenient to wear a cap and chooses not to simply out of laziness, it is makruh. If they have a medical reason (e.g., head wound), it is not makruh.
Local custom may affect the degree of dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصلاته حاسراً رأسه للتكاسل، أو للتهاون بها
[٢] قوله : للتكاسل؛ أي لأجل الكسل، بأن استثقل تغطيته ولم يرها أمراً مهماً في الصلاة، فتركها لذلك، وأصل الكسل ترك العمل؛ لعدم الإرادة، فلو لعدم القدرة فهو عجز. كذا في «شرح المنية».
And his prayer with uncovered head out of laziness or negligence regarding it. [2] His saying: 'out of laziness' - meaning due to laziness, such that he considers covering it burdensome and does not see it as important in prayer, so he omits it. The root of laziness is abandoning work due to lack of desire; if it is due to inability, that is incapacity, not laziness. Thus in Sharh al-Munyah.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This is a ruling about praying with the head uncovered (without a cap or turban). The matn states that if a person prays with uncovered head out of laziness (kasal) or negligence (tahawun), it is disliked. The commentator explains that laziness means he finds covering his head inconvenient and does not consider it important for prayer, so he leaves it off. This is distinct from incapacity: if he cannot cover his head due to a valid reason, it is not disliked. The ruling is based on the principle that one should not show negligence in prayer by omitting a customary act of respect. In the Hanafi school, it is considered makruh tanzih to pray with uncovered head when there is no excuse, because it resembles a lack of reverence. However, if local custom treats head covering as a mark of respect, then leaving it off may be more severely disliked.

Key Terms
حاسر الرأس (hāṣir al-ra's): Uncovering the head, i.e., not wearing a cap, turban, or similar cover.
كسل (kasal): Laziness; lack of willingness to perform an action due to indifference.
تهاون (tahāwun): Negligence; making light of a religious matter.

Ruling on Images in the Prayer Area
States the ruling and exceptions for images in prayer area

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on images in the prayer area, identify the exceptions for images behind or underfoot, and explain the legal basis from hadith.

Core Idea
An image underfoot is not disliked; an image in front makes prayer makruh tahrimi.

The presence of images in the prayer space raises questions of reverence and idolatry. This passage from a Hanafi commentary clarifies the ruling based on the image's location and the principle of humiliation (imtihan). Understanding these nuances is essential for fulfilling the obligation of prayer with proper reverence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the act of performing prayer while an image of a living being is present in the prayer area. The ruling depends on the image's position relative to the worshipper.
The image must be of a living being (human or animal), not a lifeless object.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is the hadith: 'Angels do not enter a house containing a dog or an image.' This is an ahad hadith, so it provides zanni evidence.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, Ahmad, Ibn Hibban, and others.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith indicates a general prohibition of images in houses. However, the Hanafi school interprets the prohibition as makruh tahrimi (close to haram) rather than haram due to the exceptions for humiliated items like carpets and pillows, which are mentioned in other versions of the hadith.
The key is the principle of istihsan: what is humiliated (mumtahan) is not accorded veneration.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools, such as the Shafi'i, consider it haram to pray with an image of a living being in front, based on the same hadith but without accepting the exceptions to the same degree.
The Hanafi school's use of istihsan and the concept of humiliation is distinctive.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is that prayer is makruh tahrimi if a living image is in front, beside, or above; it is not disliked if the image is behind or underfoot, especially on items that are trodden upon.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is consistently held in later Hanafi works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمامه، أو بحذائه، أو في السقف أو معلّقة، فإن كانت خلفه، أو تحت قدميه لا يُكْرَه. ولا تكره لو كانت خلفه، أو على بساط يداس ويوطء إلا إذا كانت في موضع سجوده. والأصل في هذا أنّ كلَّ ما كان فيه تشبّه بعبادة الأوثان أو وجد فيه تعظيم الصورة تكره فيه الصلاة، وما لا فلا، وإن كان وضعُ الصورة في البيت ممنوعاً مطلقاً إلا في البساط والوسادة وغيرهما مما يمتهن ويهان. والأصل فيه حديث: «لا تدخل الملائكةُ بيتاً فيه كلب ولا صورة»، أخرجه أبو داود والنسائي وابن ماجه وأحمد وابن حبان وغيرهم، والحديث مُخرج في الصحيحين بألفاظ متقاربة، وفيهما ما يدلّ على جوازها في الفراش ونحوه.
In front of him, or beside him, or on the ceiling, or hung; if it is behind him or under his feet, it is not disliked. And it is not disliked if it is behind him or on a carpet that is trodden and stepped on, unless it is in the place of his prostration. The basis for this is that everything that involves resemblance to idol worship or exaltation of the image, prayer is disliked therein, otherwise not. Although placing an image in the house is absolutely prohibited except on carpets, pillows, and other things that are humiliated and disrespected. The basis for it is the hadith: 'Angels do not enter a house containing a dog or an image,' narrated by Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, Ahmad, Ibn Hibban, and others. The hadith is recorded in the Sahihs with similar wordings, and therein is evidence for its permissibility on bedding and the like.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on images in the prayer area. It first lists the prohibited positions: if a living image is in front, beside, or above the worshipper, prayer is makruh tahrimi (close to haram). The key exception: if the image is behind or underfoot, there is no dislike. Even if on a carpet that is walked on, it is excepted unless directly under the prostration spot. The legal basis is two-fold: first, images of living beings resemble idolatry, and second, the hadith prohibits images in houses with an exception for items of lowly use (carpets, pillows). This aligns with the Hanafi principle that what is humiliated is not accorded veneration.

Key Terms
صورة (surah): An image of a living being (animal or human), not a lifeless object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the ruling on images in prayer depends on their location: if in front or above, prayer is makruh tahrimi; if behind or underfoot, it is not disliked, due to the principle of humiliation.

Praying Bareheaded: Laziness vs. Humility
Distinguishes between disliked and commendable bareheaded prayer based on intent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between disliked bareheaded prayer (due to laziness) and commendable bareheaded prayer (for humility), and apply the ruling to scenarios including a fallen cap.

Core Idea
Praying bareheaded is disliked only if due to laziness; if for humility, it is commendable.

When a person prays with his head uncovered, the ruling depends entirely on his intention. If he omits covering out of laziness or negligence, the prayer is valid but makruh tanzihi (disliked). However, if he uncovers his head to express humility and submissiveness before Allah, the act becomes commendable, reflecting the khushu' praised in the Quran. This lesson unpacks the textual basis and the practical distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its variable rulings
The act is prayer with uncovered head. Recognize that the hukm changes based on the underlying intention: 1) laziness → makruh tanzihi; 2) negligence → makruh tanzihi; 3) humility → commendable (no dislike).
The commentator explicitly contrasts 'laziness' (feeling it burdensome) with 'incapacity' (inability), which is excused.
2
Locate the proof for the commendable case
The text cites Quran 23:1-2 praising those who are humble (khashi'un) in prayer. This verse serves as a dalil that expressing humility outwardly, including through uncovering the head, is permissible and even praiseworthy.
The proof is a general virtue, not a specific command to uncover the head.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (indication)
The verse praises khushu', which is primarily an internal state. The commentator argues that showing its external effects is not blameworthy. Thus, uncovering the head for humility is a legitimate manifestation of khushu'.
The dalala is zanni (probable) because the verse is general and does not mention bareheadedness specifically.
4
Note the internal dispute on preference
There is a difference of opinion on whether it is preferable to uncover for humility or to remain covered. Both positions are rooted in the same objective of humility, differing only in how it is best expressed.
The text does not resolve the dispute; both views are considered valid.
5
Apply the ruling to the fallen cap scenario
If one's head covering falls during prayer, it is recommended (mustahab) to pick it up and replace it, provided it does not involve excessive movement. Excessive movement would invalidate the prayer.
This shows the default is to cover, but need for renewal if disrupted, balanced against prayer's integrity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصلاته حاسراً رأسه للتكاسل، أو للتهاون بها. ثم قوله: للتكاسل؛ أي لأجل الكسل، بأن استثقل تغطيته ولم يرها أمراً مهماً في الصلاة، فتركها لذلك، وأصل الكسل ترك العمل؛ لعدم الإرادة، فلو لعدم القدرة فهو عجز. ثم قوله: لا للتذلل؛ أي لقصدِ التذلّل وإظهار الخشوع؛ فإنّ الخشوع في الصلاة أمر مستحسن، مدح الله به أقواماً فقال: {قَدْ أَفْلَحَ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ الَّذِينَ هُمْ فِي صَلَاتِهِمْ خَشِعُونَ}، وهو وإن كان من أفعال القلب لكن لا بأس بإظهار آثاره في الظاهر وهل الأولى ترك كشف الرأس للتذلل أو فعله فيه قولان. فلو سقطت قلنسوته في الصلاة فاعادتها أحسن إلا إذا احتاج إلى عمل كثير.
And his prayer with his head uncovered due to laziness or negligence. Then his statement: 'due to laziness' means because of indolence, that he finds covering it burdensome and does not consider it important in prayer, so he leaves it for that. The origin of laziness is leaving action due to lack of will; if due to inability, it is incapacity. Then his statement: 'not for humility' means intending humility and displaying submissiveness; indeed, humility in prayer is commendable, Allah praised people for it by saying: 'Successful are the believers who are in their prayers humble.' Although it is an act of the heart, there is no harm in manifesting its effects outwardly. There are two opinions on whether it is preferable to leave the head uncovered for humility or to do so. If his cap falls during prayer, replacing it is recommended unless it requires excessive action.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling of praying bareheaded (kasr al-ra's). The base text states it is disliked if done out of laziness (forgetfulness of importance) or negligence (indifference to the prayer's excellence). The commentary clarifies: laziness means deliberately not covering due to inertia, not due to inability. Negligence means lack of care for the prayer, but not outright contempt (which would be kufr). The dislikedness is tanzihi (disliked but not sinful). Then the passage distinguishes a case where one uncovers for humility (tadhallul) — this is commendable because it shows submissiveness before Allah, akin to the humility described in the Quran (Surah al-Mu'minun:1-2). However, there is a difference of opinion whether this act is preferable or not. The ruling on a fallen cap: if one's cap falls during prayer, it is better to put it back on unless doing so involves excessive movement.

Key Terms
تكاسل (takasul): Laziness leading to omission of covering the head due to feeling it burdensome.
تهاون (tahawun): Negligence or lack of care for the prayer, but not contempt (which is kufr).
تذلل (tadhallul): Humility and submission to Allah by uncovering the head.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man prays without a hat because he feels it is too hot and bothersome to put one on, and he just wants to finish prayer quickly, what is the ruling?
A) Prayer is invalid
B) Prayer is makruh tanzihi due to laziness
C) Prayer is mustahab because he is showing humility
D) No problem at all

Key Takeaway
The ruling on praying bareheaded hinges on intention: laziness invokes dislike, humility removes it.

Prayer in Ordinary Work Clothes: When Is It Disliked?
Explains the ruling on praying in thiyab al-badhlah with Quranic proof and condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying in thiyab al-badhlah and explain the condition that modifies the dislike.

Core Idea
Praying in everyday work clothes is makruh tanzihi only if better clothes are available; otherwise, no dislike.

You come home from work, still in your everyday clothes, and it's time for prayer. Should you change? The Hanafi school offers a nuanced answer that balances reverence for prayer with practical circumstances. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof from the Quran, and the key condition that many Muslims overlook.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific act being ruled upon? (Prayer in thiyab al-badhlah). What is the hukm level? (makruh tanzihi). Note that this is a ruling of dislike, not prohibition.
The Arabic term 'badhlah' refers to clothes worn for everyday work and service, not for visiting dignitaries.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites Quran 4:142 about hypocrites praying lazily. Identify the verse and its context. Consider how it serves as evidence for dislike.
The verse does not explicitly mention clothing; the connection is through the concept of laziness and lack of reverence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the verse proves the ruling: wearing ordinary work clothes when one has better attire shows a lack of care and reverence, resembling the lazy attitude of hypocrites. This is the logical link (wajh al-dalalah) from the text to the ruling.
The dislike is connected to the intention and attitude, not the clothing itself.
4
Identify the condition
The text explicitly states: 'This applies when he has other clothes; otherwise not.' Identify this condition and understand its effect. The ruling changes based on availability.
This condition shows that the dislike is not absolute but depends on the person's situation.
5
Note the recommended practice and common error
The author mentions that the recommended dress for prayer includes a shirt, lower garment, and turban. Also, it is not disliked to pray with only a skullcap, contrary to common belief among laypeople.
The passage dismisses a widespread misconception about the skullcap being disliked.

Source Text

And [prayer] in ordinary work clothes (thiyab al-badhlah), which are worn at home and not worn when visiting dignitaries. It appears that the dislike here is tanzihi. Its basis is taken from the verse of the Almighty regarding the hypocrites: 'And when they stand up for prayer, they stand lazily.' His statement: 'and in ordinary work clothes' means that his prayer in ordinary work clothes is disliked – with a kasra on the ba and sukun on the dhal – meaning clothing of daily use and service. The dislike is tanzihi, as in al-Bahr. The reason for the dislike is neglect of attention to prayer, which is the best of acts of worship. This applies when he has other clothes; otherwise not. They have mentioned that it is recommended to pray in a shirt, lower garment, and turban. Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on praying in thiyab al-badhlah – clothes worn for everyday household work, not for going out to meet important people. The ruling: it is makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked) if one has better clothes available. The proof is the Quranic verse condemning hypocrites who prayed lazily, implying that prayer in ordinary clothes shows a lack of reverence. The dislike is removed if one does not possess better clothes. The passage also notes that the recommended dress for prayer is a shirt, lower garment, and turban. It specifically dismisses a common folk belief that praying with only a skullcap (without a turban) is disliked; this is not correct. Similarly, the idea that if the imam wears only a cap while the followers have turbans, the prayer is disliked, is false.

Key Terms
ثياب البذلة (thiyab al-badhlah): Everyday work clothes, not formal or dignified attire.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Prayer is invalid because clothes are dirty
B) Prayer is makruh tanzihi
C) No dislike because he has no other clothes
D) Prayer is haram

Key Takeaway
The dislike of praying in everyday work clothes is conditional: it applies only when one has better clothes and neglects them; otherwise, the prayer is fine.

Dislike of Praying in Work Clothes
Ruling on praying in humble attire and intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on praying in work clothes (thiyab al-badhlah) and explain the Hanafi proof from Surah al-Nisa:142.

Core Idea
Praying in work clothes is mild disliked if better clothes available, based on hypocrites' laziness verse.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the Hanafi ruling on praying in humble attire often worn at home. While some may think it is always disrespectful, the text clarifies that the dislike is mild and conditional. The key proof is the Quranic verse about hypocrites who stand lazily for prayer, drawing a parallel to lack of reverence in dress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act being discussed (praying in work clothes) and its legal classification (makruh tanzihi).
The text explicitly says 'الكراهة تنزيهية'.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: the Quranic verse (Surah al-Nisa:142) describing hypocrites as lazy.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the verse proves the ruling: laziness in dress reflects lack of reverence, similar to the hypocrites' attitude.
The link is the shared illa of minimal care for prayer.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Recognize that some scholars may not consider it disliked at all.
The text says 'والظاهر أن الكراهة هاهنا تنزيهية' indicating it's the apparent view.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Understand the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position: makruh tanzihi if one has other clothes; otherwise no dislike.
This is the ruling adopted by later Hanafi authorities.

Not for showing humility, and in clothes of work (i.e., the intention is not heedlessness which is insult, for that is disbelief, but rather minimal care and preservation of its limits. 'Not for showing humility' and 'in clothes of work' – and it is what is worn in the house and not worn to go to dignitaries. It appears that the dislike here is tanzihi (mildly disliked), and its justification is taken from His statement concerning the hypocrites: 'And when they stand up for prayer, they stand up lazily'. So if his cap falls during prayer, replacing it is better unless it requires much action. Likewise in Fatawa al-Hajja and al-Tatarkhaniyya. 1) His statement: 'for it' meaning insult to prayer and belittling it and considering it insignificant. (2) His statement: 'not for showing humility' i.e., intending humility and displaying reverence, for reverence in prayer is commendable, Allah praised people for it: 'Successful indeed are the believers who are humble in their prayer'. Although it is an act of the heart, there is no harm in manifesting its effects outwardly. There are two opinions on whether it is better to leave the head uncovered for humility or to do it. (3) His statement: 'and in clothes of work' i.e., his prayer in work clothes is disliked – with kasra of the ba and sukun of the dhal – meaning commonness and service. The dislike is tanzihi, as in al-Bahr. The reason for the dislike is lack of heed to prayer which is the best of acts. This is if he has other clothes; otherwise no. If not, then no dislike. They mentioned that it is recommended to pray in a shirt, waist wrap, and turban. It is not disliked to suffice with a cap, and no attention is paid to what is famous among the common people of dislike of that, and similarly what is famous that if the follower has a turban and the imam suffices with a cap, it is disliked.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing the dislike (karahah) of praying in work clothes (thiyab al-badhlah) and clarifying the intention of humility (tawadhu'). The commentator explains that the intended meaning is not heedlessness (tahawun) which is disbelief, but rather minimal care. The ruling is that praying in such clothes is makruh tanzihi (mildly disliked) if one has other clothes; otherwise it is not disliked. The proof is the Quranic verse about hypocrites standing lazily for prayer. Additionally, there is discussion about replacing a fallen cap: it is better to replace it unless it requires excessive movement. There is also a discussion about the intention of tawadhu' – whether to uncover the head for humility – which has two opinions. The preferred practice is to pray in a shirt, waist wrap, and turban, and it is not disliked to suffice with a cap, contrary to common belief.

Key Terms
ثياب البذلة (thiyab al-badhlah): Clothes worn for common service and housework, not for going out to dignitaries.
الكراهة التنزيهية (al-karahah al-tanzihiyyah): A level of dislike that is close to permissible; not sinful, but better to avoid.

Praying in work clothes is makruh tanzihi in the Hanafi school if one has other clothes, based on the Quranic analogy to hypocrites' laziness.

Wiping Dust from Forehead in Prayer
Ruling on wiping the forehead during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wiping the forehead during prayer and explain the hadith-based proof that renders it makruh tanzih.

Core Idea
Wiping dust from the forehead while praying is disliked because any unnecessary movement distracts from khushu'.

This lesson focuses on a specific act related to prayer: wiping dust from the forehead. The Hanafi school classifies this as makruh tanzih (mildly disliked). The proof comes from a prophetic prohibition against wiping pebbles while praying, which is extended by analogy to include similar distracting actions. Understanding this ruling helps us appreciate the emphasis on focus and stillness during salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the act is 'wiping the forehead from dust' and the ruling is 'makruh tanzih' in the Hanafi school.
The ruling is from the Hanafi school, based on the hadith analogy.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith cited: 'Do not wipe the pebbles while you are praying.' It is narrated by the six major hadith collectors.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but binding for fiqh rulings.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the prohibition of wiping pebbles is applied to wiping dust from the forehead through qiyas (analogy). Both are unnecessary movements that distract from prayer.
The illa (effective cause) is the distracting movement, not the specific object.
4
Understand the hukm level
The prohibition is karahah tanzihiyyah (mildly disliked), not haram, because the hadith is zanni in both transmission and meaning.
The prayer remains valid but is deficient in focus.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, a worshipper should avoid wiping dust during prayer. If done, the prayer is still valid but the action is disliked.
Some exceptions exist for extreme discomfort, but the general rule is dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And wiping his forehead from dust in it. [1] His statement: 'and wiping his forehead'; the Messenger of Allah said: 'Do not wipe the pebbles while you are praying' – narrated by the authors of the six books.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof regarding wiping the forehead (or face) from dust during prayer. The commentator cites the hadith prohibiting wiping pebbles (حصى) while praying, analogously including wiping dust from the forehead. The action is disliked (makruh) because it distracts from the prayer. The hadith is from the six major collections.

Key Terms
مسح الجبهة (masḥ al-jabhat): The act of wiping the forehead to remove dust during prayer.

The Ruling on Looking Up to the Sky in Prayer
Ruling on raising eyes to the sky during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on looking at the sky during prayer and explain the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
Looking up to the sky in prayer is makruh tanzih because it violates khushu.

The Hanafi school teaches that prayer requires full humility (khushu). One act that disrupts this is raising the eyes to the sky. This lesson examines the ruling, its proof from a famous hadith, and the reasoning behind the mild dislike.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
State exactly what act is being ruled upon and the hukm level (fard, wajib, sunnah, makruh, haram). Here it is looking at the sky, which is makruh tanzih.
Makruh tanzih means mildly disliked; avoiding it earns reward but doing it does not incur sin.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is a hadith where the Prophet (PBUH) asked a rhetorical question and warned that eyesight could be snatched.
The hadith is narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and others, making it authentic.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly prohibits the act with a stern threat, indicating a strong dislike. Hanafi usul classifies such a threat as makruh tanzih because the act does not invalidate prayer.
The warning shows it is not minor, but the ruling is not haram because the act does not meet the criteria for prohibition.
4
Understand the illa (reason)
Looking up causes abandonment of khushu and is bad adab. The illa is the loss of humility, which is wajib in prayer.
This helps apply the ruling to similar distractions.
5
Apply to prayer
During prayer, keep your gaze fixed on the place of sujud. Avoid looking up or around unnecessarily.
The ruling applies to both fard and nafl prayers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنظر إلى السماء، [٢] قوله : والنظر إلى السماء ؛ لأنَّ فيه ترك الخشوع وسوء الأدب، وقد قال النبي : ما بال أقوام يرفعون أبصارهم إلى السماء في صلاتهم»)، وقال: «لينتهنّ عن ذلك أو لتخطفن أبصارهم» (۳) ، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم ، وأبو داود وغيرهم بألفاظ متقاربة.
And looking at the sky. [2] His statement: 'and looking at the sky' – because it involves abandoning humility and bad manners, and the Prophet said: 'What is the matter with people who raise their eyes to the sky in their prayer?' and said: 'Let them desist from that or their eyesight will be snatched away.' Narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud and others with similar wordings.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This ruling with proof addresses looking at the sky during prayer. It is disliked (makruh) because it violates humility (khushu) and is considered bad adab (etiquette). The proof is from two hadith: the Prophet's rhetorical question and stern warning that those who do so risk losing their eyesight. The hadith is narrated by major hadith collectors.

Key Terms
الخشوع (al-khushu): Humility and attentive focus in prayer; an essential quality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling for looking at the sky during prayer?
A) Haram
B) Makruh Tanzih
C) Mustahab
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Looking up to the sky during prayer is makruh tanzih due to the loss of khushu and the explicit warning of the Prophet (PBUH).
